Tafwid al-Talaq: Tamlik with the Meaning of Ta'liq
Defines tafwid as tamlik with ta'liq through critique of alternative views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of tafwid al-talaq, explain its dual nature as tamlik and ta'liq, and apply the ruling that revocation is invalid.

Core Idea
Tafwid is tamlik with the meaning of ta'liq — both ownership and suspension combine.

Tafwid al-talaq is a unique delegation of divorce where the husband authorizes his wife to divorce herself. The classical Hanafi texts debate its true nature: is it a pure transfer of ownership (tamlik) or a suspended divorce (ta'liq)? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq resolves the issue by synthesizing both views, establishing that tafwid partakes of both tamlik and ta'liq, which has direct consequences on its validity and the husband's ability to revoke.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal nature in dispute
The passage discusses whether tafwid is pure tamlik (complete by husband alone) or like an oath (suspension). Recognize that this affects the ruling on revocation.
Tamlik makes completion in council; ta'liq makes it extend beyond.
2
Analyze the criticism of the pure tamlik view
Some argued tafwid is like an oath because it entails suspension on wife's action. But that would apply to all agencies, which is invalid. Understand why the criticism is rejected.
The objection is that agency cannot have suspended permission.
3
Understand the synthetic view
The correct view is tamlik with the meaning of ta'liq. This means tafwid has aspects of both: completion in council due to tamlik, but remaining effective beyond due to ta'liq.
This resolves the earlier inconsistency.
4
Apply the consequences
Because of tamlik, the husband cannot revoke during the council; because of ta'liq, the delegation persists after the council, and the husband cannot revoke at all.
Revocation is invalid in all stages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار بعدم ذكر قبولها إلى أنه تمليك يتم بالمملك وحده، فلو رجع قبل انقضاء المجلس لم يصح، وما علل به في الذخيرة من كونه بمعنى اليمين إذ هو تعليق الطلاق بتطليقها نفسها فخلاف التحقيق لأنه اعتبار ممكن في سائر الوكالات لتضمنه معنى إذا بعته فقد أجزته فكان يقتضي أن لا يصح الرجوع عنها أنه مع صحيح . كذا في فتح القدير وفيه نظر، لأن هذا الاعتبار لا يمكن في الوكالة لأنه لا يصح تعليق الإجازة بالشرط كما في الكنز وغيره بخلاف الطلاق فكان سهواً. والحق ما في الذخيرة وفي جامع الفصولين أنه تمليك فيه معنى التعليق فلكونه تمليكاً تقيد بالمجلس، ولكونه تعليقاً بقي إلى ما وراء المجلس ولم يصح الرجوع عنه عملاً بشبهيه.
And by his not mentioning her acceptance, he indicated that it is a tamlik that is completed by the owner alone, so if he revoked before the end of the council, it is not valid. And what was reasoned in al-Dhakhira – that it is like an oath, as it is a suspension of divorce on her divorcing herself – is contrary to verification, because that consideration is possible in all agencies, as it entails the meaning of 'if I sell it to you, then I have permitted it', which would preclude valid revocation. Thus in Fath al-Qadir, and there is consideration: because this consideration is not possible in agency, as suspension of permission on a condition is not valid, as in al-Kanz and others, unlike divorce. So it was an oversight. The truth is what is in al-Dhakhira and in Jami' al-Fusulayn: that it is tamlik with the meaning of suspension. Because it is tamlik, it is restricted to the council; because it is suspension, it remains beyond the council and revocation is not valid, acting on both similarities.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of tafwid. The author notes that acceptance is not mentioned, indicating that tamlik is completed by the husband alone, so his revocation before the council ends is invalid. Some argued that it is like an oath (suspension), but that view is criticized. The correct view, according to al-Dhakhira and Jami' al-Fusulayn, is that tafwid is tamlik with an element of ta'liq (suspension). This dual nature explains why it is restricted to the council (due to its tamlik aspect) but extends beyond the council (due to its ta'liq aspect) and why revocation is invalid. The passage resolves a scholarly dispute and presents the relied-upon view.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of the divorce on a future condition; here, the wife's act of divorcing herself is the condition.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; in tafwid, it means the husband gives the wife ownership of the right to divorce.
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation of divorce; the husband authorizes the wife to divorce herself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, what is the nature of tafwid al-talaq?
A) Pure tamlik
B) Pure ta'liq
C) Tamlik with the meaning of ta'liq
D) It is wakalah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi relied-upon view is that tafwid al-talaq is tamlik with the meaning of ta'liq, combining both aspects—a ruling that governs the validity and revocability of the delegation.

Tafwid al-Talaq: Agency or Ownership? The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Dispute on tafwid as agency vs ownership and nuance of specifying 'choose divorce'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two positions on whether tafwid is wakalah, explain why the correct view rejects agency, and apply the exception when the husband specifies 'choose divorce'.

Core Idea
Tafwid gives ownership, not agency; the husband cannot revoke, and divorce is ba'in unless specified otherwise.

When a husband delegates the power of divorce to his wife, a critical question arises: is this an appointment as an agent (wakalah) or a transfer of ownership (tamlik)? The Hanafi school contains a dispute on this very point. In this lesson, we will examine the two views, the reasoning for the preferred position, and a crucial exception that changes the type of divorce from irrevocable to revocable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
From the passage, note the first view: tafwid is wakalah, meaning the husband retains the right to dismiss the wife's authority. Then identify the correct view: it is not wakalah, and the husband cannot dismiss her. The divorce that occurs is ba'in (irrevocable).
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; some early Hanafi scholars held the agency view.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The passage states the reason: tafwid implies 'extraction and purity from that ownership' (الاستخلاص والصفا من ذلك الملك). If the divorce were revocable (raj'i), the benefit of delegation would be lost because the husband could take her back against her will. Thus, the divorce must be ba'in to give effect to the delegation.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on the purpose of the delegation.
3
Understand the exception for specification
The passage then adds a key nuance: if the husband specifies 'choose divorce' (اختياري الطلاق) or says 'your affair is in your hand' (أمرك بيدك) in the context of one revocable divorce, then the wife's choice results in a single raj'i divorce. This is because the specification limits her choice and indicates the husband's intent for revocability.
This exception is derived from al-Bada'i' and the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq.
4
Determine the practical ruling
Summarize: For absolute tafwid, the divorce is ba'in. For tafwid with specification like 'choose a divorce', the divorce is raj'i. This distinction has practical consequences for whether the husband can take her back during the iddah.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if agency view were accepted, the husband could revoke the delegation; but under the correct view, he cannot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي جامع الفصولين : تفويض الطلاق إليها قيل هو وكالة يملك عزلها والأصح أنه لا يملكه ا هـ . وإنما وقع البائن به لأنه ينبئ عن الاستخلاص والصفا من ذلك الملك وهو بالبينونة وإلا لم تحصل فائدة التخيير إذ كان له أن يراجعها شاءت أو أبت. وقيد باقتصاره على التخيير المطلق لأنه لو قال لها اختاري الطلاق فقالت اختارت الطلاق فهي واحدة رجعية لأنه لما صرح فقد خيرها بين نفسها بتطليقة واحدة رجعية وبين ترك التطليقة وكذا في قوله أمرك بيدك . كذا في البدائع وهو مستفاد من قول المصنف آخر الباب اختاري تطليقة أو أمرك بيدك في تطليقة.
And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: Delegation of divorce to her: it is said it is agency, and he has the right to dismiss her; the correct view is that he does not have the right to dismiss her. And [the divorce] occurs as ba'in because it implies extraction and purity from that ownership, and it is by ba'inuna; otherwise, the benefit of delegation would not be achieved since he could take her back whether she wished or not. And he restricted it to absolute delegation, because if he said to her 'choose divorce' and she said 'I choose divorce', then it is one revocable divorce, because when he specified, he gave her the choice between herself with one revocable divorce and leaving the divorce. Similarly, in his saying 'your affair is in your hand'. Thus in al-Bada'i', and it is derived from the author's statement at the end of the chapter: 'choose a divorce' or 'your affair is in your hand in one divorce'.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute: some say tafwid is wakalah (agency), allowing the husband to dismiss her; the correct view is that he cannot dismiss her. The divorce that occurs is ba'in (irrevocable) because the purpose of delegation is to give her full control, which would be negated if the husband could take her back. However, if the husband specifies 'choose divorce' (ikhtari al-talaq), and she chooses, it is a single revocable divorce because the specification limits her choice. Similarly, if he says 'your affair is in your hand' (amruk bi-yadik), the same rule applies. This is from al-Bada'i' and derived from the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Key Terms
بائن (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately, without right of return during iddah.
وكالة (wakalah): Agency; here, the view that delegation is appointment of the wife as agent.
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation of the power of divorce to the wife, often by saying 'amruk bi-yadik' or similar.
رجعة (raj'ah): Revocable divorce; the husband can take her back during iddah without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife, 'I delegate to you the power to choose divorce,' and she chooses it, what type of divorce occurs?
A) Ba'in
B) Raj'i
C) Three divorces
D) It is invalid

Key Takeaway
Tafwid is tamlik not wakalah; the husband cannot revoke, and the default divorce is ba'in, but specifying 'choose one divorce' makes it raj'i.

Effect of Husband's Acceptance on Wife's Divorce Statement
Rulings on wife's divorce statement and husband's acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife says 'I divorced myself' and the husband accepts, and distinguish between explicit and implicit statements regarding intention.

Core Idea
Husband's acceptance transforms wife's statement into a valid divorce, treating it as his initiation.

In Hanafi law, a wife's unilateral statement of divorce is generally ineffective. However, if the husband subsequently accepts (ijazah), the divorce becomes valid. This lesson explores the scenario from Talkhis al-Jami', where the wife says 'tallaqtu nafsi' and the husband says 'ajaztu', and the resulting rulings including the role of intention and the distinction between explicit (sarih) and implicit (kinayah) statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The wife says 'tallaqtu nafsi' (I divorced myself), and the husband says 'ajaztu' (I accept). According to the text, the divorce occurs (tallaqat), treating the husband's acceptance as an initiation (insha').
This is the core ruling: the husband's acceptance perfects the divorce.
2
Determine the effect of intention for multiple divorces
If both parties intend three divorces, the divorce occurs as ba'in (irrevocable), unlike the default case (first) where it is one revocable divorce.
Intention matters only for kinayah, not for explicit statements.
3
Distinguish explicit (sarih) from implicit (kinayah)
The text contrasts 'tallaqtu' (sarih) with 'harramtu' (kinayah). For sarih, no intention of divorce is required, and intention of three is invalid. For kinayah, intention is necessary; the husband's 'ajaztu' is like 'harramtu', requiring divorce intent to be effective.
Without divorce intent, 'harramtu' + 'ajaztu' becomes ila' (vow of abstinence).
4
Examine the correction of oversight
The author notes an earlier oversight: if the wife says 'akhtartu nafsi la bal zawji' (I choose myself, no rather my husband), some thought it does not occur due to idrab (disruption), but the correct view is that divorce occurs.
This shows the importance of precise wording and the author's own correction.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Summarize: if a wife says 'tallaqtu nafsi' and husband accepts, one revocable divorce occurs unless both intend three, then ba'in. If she says 'harramtu' and he accepts, it requires divorce intent; otherwise it is ila'.
This is the mufta bihi position per the author and al-Nahr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي تلخيص الجامع من باب إجازة الطلاق: لو قالت طلقت نفسي فأجاز طلقت اعتباراً بالإنشاء، كذا أبنت إذا نويا ولو ثلاثاً بخلاف الأول، كذا ضمناً الاختيار ومنعوه قصداً قوله : ولو قالت اخترت نفسي لا بل زوجي يقع) قال في النهر : وما في أنه لا يقع من لأنه للإضراب عن الأول سهو اهـ. وسينبه عليه المؤلف في آخر هذا الباب قوله : (بخلاف الأول) أي قولها طلقت لأنه صريح فلم تشترط فيه النية ولم تصح فيه نية الثلاث، وكذا لو قالت حرمت عليك نفسي فقال الزوج أجزت كان كما في أبنت لكونه من الكنايات لكن هنا بدون نية الزوج يكون إيلاء والفرق أن أجزت هنا بمنزلة «حرمت» وتحريم الحلال يمين بالنص.
And in Talkhis al-Jami', from the chapter on Acceptance of Divorce: If she says 'I divorced myself' and he accepts, she is divorced, considering it as initiation (insha'). Similarly, if they intend three, [it occurs as] ba'in unlike the first [case]. Similarly, if they include the option and prevent it intentionally. His statement: 'And if she said I choose myself, no rather my husband, it occurs'. He said in al-Nahr: And what is [in some opinions] that it does not occur because it is idrab from the first is an oversight. The author will draw attention to this at the end of this chapter: 'Unlike the first', meaning her saying 'I divorced', because it is explicit (sarih), so intention is not required, and intention of three is not valid. Similarly, if she says 'I made myself forbidden to you' and the husband says 'I accept', it is like [the case of] abantu if they intend [divorce], but here without the husband's intention, it is ila', and the difference is that 'ajaztu' here is like 'harramtu', and forbidding the permissible is an oath by the text.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where the wife says 'I divorced myself' (tallaqtu nafsi) and the husband says 'I accept' (ajaztu). The divorce occurs, treating the husband's acceptance as initiating the divorce (insha'). If both intend three divorces, it occurs as ba'in, unlike the previous case where it was one. The author then comments on a previous oversight: if the wife says 'I choose myself, no rather my husband', divorce occurs, contrary to the view that idrab prevents it. The author will correct this later. Then it distinguishes between explicit statements (sarih) like 'tallaqtu' where no intention is needed and intention of three is invalid, and kinayah like 'harramtu' where intention matters. If the wife says 'I made myself forbidden' and the husband accepts, it is like divorce if intended, but without the husband's intention, it becomes ila' (vow of abstinence), because 'ajaztu' is similar to 'harramtu', which is an oath.

Key Terms
إجازة (ijazah): Acceptance or permission by the husband after the wife's statement; it makes the divorce effective ex nunc.
إيلاء (ila'): A vow by the husband to abstain from marital relations, leading to divorce if not broken within four months.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No divorce
B) One revocable divorce
C) It depends on intention
D) Irrevocable divorce

Key Takeaway
The husband's acceptance converts the wife's statement into a divorce, with nuances for explicit vs. implicit and the possibility of ila'.

Talaq — Divorce

8 min
Wife's 'Tallaqtu Nafsi' with 'fa' vs 'wa' – Effect of Husband's Acceptance
Expansion on rulings of delegated divorce and conjunction effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal effects of the wife using 'fa' versus 'wa' when saying 'tallaqtu nafsi' after delegating the affair, and state the resulting divorce rulings when the husband accepts.

Core Idea
Using 'fa' ties the divorce to the delegation, invalidating it; using 'wa' separates them, allowing both if husband accepts.

This passage explores a subtle but crucial distinction in Hanafi divorce law. When a wife declares she has made her affair in her hand and then says 'fa-tallaqtu nafsi' (so I divorced myself), the letter 'fa' makes the divorce an explanation of the delegation, potentially causing a temporal paradox. However, if she uses 'wa-tallaqtu nafsi' (and I divorced myself), the two acts are independent, and the husband's acceptance can validate both. Understanding this nuance is essential for correctly applying the rulings on delegated divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wife's initial statement
The wife says either 'ja'altu al-khiyar ilaya' (I gave the option to myself) or 'ja'altu amri bi-yadi' (I made my affair in my hand). This is a delegation (tafwid) of the power to divorce.
This delegation itself requires the husband's acceptance to become effective.
2
Determine the conjunction used before 'tallaqtu nafsi'
Is it 'fa' or 'wa'? The ruling hinges on this conjunction.
The text specifically contrasts 'fa' (al-fa' lil-tafsir) and 'wa' (al-waw lil-ibtida').
3
Analyze the 'fa' case
With 'fa', the divorce is an explanation (tafsir) of the delegation. The consideration in explanation is for the explicandum (the affair being placed in her hand). Thus, the divorce would have to occur after the affair is in her hand, but since it is uttered simultaneously, it lacks prior tamlik (ownership). Therefore, the divorce is void.
The husband's acceptance (ajaztu) only validates the delegation, not the divorce.
4
Analyze the 'wa' case
With 'wa', the two statements are independent: she performs two separate acts – delegation and divorce. Both are acts the husband can initiate (i.e., they depend on his acceptance). If he accepts, both are valid.
She gets a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) and retains the option to divorce again by virtue of the accepted delegation.
5
Compare with 'ikhtartu' scenario
If the wife simply says 'ikhtartu nafsi' (I choose myself) without prior delegation, the husband's acceptance has no effect because 'ikhtartu' is not a divorce expression unless in response to delegation.
This reinforces that the validity of the wife's statement depends on context and wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قالت اخترت نفسي منك فقال الزوج أجزت ونوى الطلاق لا يقع شيء لأن قولها «اخترت لم يوضع للطلاق لا صريحاً ولا كناية ولا عرف إيقاع الطلاق به إلا إذا وقع جواباً لتخيير الزوج. كذا لو قالت قد جعلت الخيار إلى أو قد جعلت أمري بيدي فطلقت نفسي فقال الزوج أجزت من حيث إنه لا يقع شيء لكن يصير الخيار والأمر بيدها إذا نوى الزوج الطلاق، وإنما لم يقع الطلاق بقولها فطلقت نفسي إذا أجاز الزوج لأن الفاء للتفسير والطلاق يصلح تفسيراً للتفويض والعبرة في التفسير للمفسر بالفتح وهو الأمر فكانت مطلقة قبل صيرورة الأمر بيدها فيلغو لفقد التمليك سابقاً على التطليق بخلاف الواو لأنها للابتداء لا للتفسير فكانت آتية بأمرين يملك الزوج إنشاءهما وهما التفويض والطلاق. فإذا قال أجزت جاز الأمران فتطلق رجعية وتتخير في إيقاع أخرى بحكم التفويض الذي أجازه بخلاف ما مر من قولها اخترت إذا أجازه الزوج حيث لا يفيد شيئاً، ولم يتوقف على إجازة الزوج لأنه إنما يتوقف عليها ما يكون له انشاؤه وهو التخيير كما في مسألتنا دون ما ليس له إنشاؤه كالاختيار. وقوله ولم يستند الخ جواب عما يقال لما قالت فطلقت بالفاء وقال الزوج أجزت صار الأمر بيدها مستنداً إلى وقت الجعل فتبين أنها طلقت بعدما صار الأمر بيدها فوجب أن
And if she said 'I choose myself from you' and the husband said 'I accept' and intended divorce, nothing occurs because her saying 'I choose' is not established for divorce, neither explicitly nor as a kinayah, nor is it customary to effect divorce with it, unless it is in response to the husband's delegation. Similarly, if she said 'I have given the option to myself' or 'I have made my affair in my hand' and then 'I divorced myself', and the husband said 'I accept', then as for whether anything occurs: nothing occurs, but the option and the affair become in her hand if the husband intended divorce. And the divorce does not occur by her saying 'I divorced myself' when the husband accepts because 'fa' is for explanation (tafsir), and divorce is suitable as an explanation of tafwid, and the consideration in explanation is for the explicandum (al-mufassar) which is al-amr (the affair), so she would have been divorced before the affair came into her hand, so it becomes void due to lack of tamlik prior to the divorce, unlike 'wa' because it is for beginning (ibtida') not explanation, so she brings two acts that the husband can initiate: tafwid and divorce. So when he says 'I accept', both are valid, so she is divorced revocably and retains the option to effect another divorce by virtue of the delegation he accepted, unlike the case where she said 'I choose' and he accepted, which gives nothing. And it does not depend on the husband's acceptance because what depends on acceptance is what he can initiate, which is tafwid, as in our case, not what he cannot initiate, like ikhtiyar. And his statement 'and it is not based...' is a response to what is said: when she said 'fatalaqtu' with 'fa' and the husband said 'ajaztu', the affair comes into her hand retrospectively to the time of the delegation, so it appears she divorced after the affair came into her hand, so it must be that...
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage compares two scenarios. First, if the wife says 'I choose myself' (ikhtartu nafsi) without prior delegation, the husband's acceptance (ajaztu) with intention of divorce gives no divorce because 'ikhtartu' is not established as a divorce expression unless in response to delegation. Second, if she says 'I have made the option to myself' or 'I have made my affair in my hand' and then says 'I divorced myself' (fa-tallaqtu nafsi), the husband's acceptance gives her the option (the delegation becomes valid), but the divorce itself does not occur because 'fa' indicates explanation; thus the divorce would have to occur after the delegation is established, but since it was uttered simultaneously, it is invalid. If she uses 'wa' (and) instead of 'fa', she is performing two separate acts: delegation and divorce, both of which the husband can accept. If he accepts, both are valid, resulting in a revocable divorce and the wife retains the option for another divorce. The passage ends mid-sentence discussing a response to a possible objection.

Key Terms
الفاء للتفسير (al-fa' lil-tafsir): The letter 'fa' used for explanation, making the following clause an interpretation of the preceding.
الواو للابتداء (al-waw lil-ibtida'): The letter 'wa' used for beginning a new independent statement.
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation of the power to divorce from husband to wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a wife says 'I have made my affair in my hand' and then 'and I divorced myself' (using 'wa'), and the husband accepts, what happens?
A) No divorce, no option
B) Option only, no divorce
C) One revocable divorce and option for more
D) Irrevocable divorce

Key Takeaway
The validity of a wife's self-divorce after delegation hinges on the conjunction: 'fa' invalidates the divorce due to temporal paradox, while 'wa' allows both acts upon the husband's acceptance.

5 min
The Phrases 'حرمت' and 'اخترت' in Tafwid al-Talaq
Rulings on the phrases 'حرمت' and 'اخترت' in delegated divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a wife saying 'حرمت' without intention (ila') and for 'اخترت' (no divorce).

Core Idea
Without proper delegation, 'حرمت' is an oath of ila', and 'اخترت' is merely a response that cannot effect divorce.

In delegated divorce, the words used by the wife must indicate her act of divorcing herself. This lesson examines two common but legally distinct phrases: 'حرمت' (she is forbidden) and 'اخترت' (I chose). Understanding their legal effects is crucial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase
Note the exact wording 'حرمت' and 'اخترت'.
2
Determine intent for 'حرمت'
Check whether the wife intended divorce. Without intention, it becomes ila'.
3
Apply the rule for 'اخترت'
Recognize that 'اخترت' is not a word of divorce and therefore has no effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حرمت وبدون النية إيلاء لأنه يمين، وفي اخترت لا يقع إذ لا وضع أصلاً ولا عرف إلا جواباً، كذا جعلت الخيار إلي أو أمري بيدي فطلقت لأن الفاء للتفسير فاعتبر المفسر ولغا لفقد التمليك سابقاً بخلاف الواو لأنه للإبتداء فتقع رجعية وتتخير إذ يوقف ماله إنشاؤه وهو التخيير دون الاختيار ولم يستند لأنه سبب عند الإجازة للتعليق بها فاعتبر المجلس بعدها. ولم يقيد بوجود الشرط قبلها في تعليق الفضولي بخلاف البيع لأنه لا يقبل التعليق فاعتبر سبباً حال العقد. كذا جعلت أمس أمري بيدي وفي قلت أمس أمري بيدي اليوم لا خيار لها لأن الوقت ثم للجعل والمجلس بعد الإجازة وهناك للأمر فانتهى بمضيه ا هـ
She is forbidden (to him) and without intention it is an oath of ila' because it is an oath. As for 'اخترت', it does not occur (divorce) because there is no literal meaning nor custom except as a response. Similarly, if she said 'I made the choice to me' or 'my affair is in my hand' and then said 'so I am divorced' (fa-tallaqt), because the فاء is for explanation, so the explained (mufassar) is considered and the (first) is void due to lack of prior tamlik. Contrary to the واو, because it is for beginning, so it falls as a raj'i divorce and she has the option, because its effect is created, which is takhyir (giving choice), not ikhtiyar (choosing). And it does not become effective retrospectively because it is a cause at the time of acceptance, contingent upon it, so the session after acceptance is considered. And it is not conditioned by the existence of a condition before it in the delegation of a third party (fuduli), contrary to sale because sale does not accept suspension, so it is considered as a cause at the time of the contract. Similarly, if she said 'yesterday my affair is in my hand', and if she said 'I said yesterday my affair is in my hand today', she has no option because the time there is for the making (ja'l) and the session is after acceptance, and here it is for the command, so it ends with its passing. End.
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the legal effects of different wordings in delegated divorce (tafwid al-talaq). It begins by addressing the phrase 'حرمت' (she is forbidden) without intention of divorce, which becomes an oath of ila' (abstention). The word 'اخترت' (I chose) as a mere response does not effect a divorce. The core discussion contrasts two common constructions: if the wife says 'جعلت الخيار إلي' (I made the choice to me) followed by 'فطلقت' (so I am divorced), the 'fa' (فاء) renders the second phrase explanatory (tafsir), so the divorce is based on 'طلقت' and the initial phrase is void due to lack of prior tamlik (delegation). However, if the conjunction is 'wa' (واو), it indicates a new beginning (ibtida'), so the divorce is raj'i (revocable) and the wife retains the option (takhyir). The author distinguishes between takhyir (the husband's act of giving choice) and ikhtiyar (the wife's act of choosing). The passage also clarifies that in the case of a third party (fuduli) acting without prior authority, the validity depends on the session after ratification. A temporal qualification like 'أمس' (yesterday) limits the option to that time, so if she says 'أمس أمري بيدي' and the husband later ratifies, no divorce occurs because the time has passed. These rulings highlight the Hanafi emphasis on precise wording and context in divorce delegation.

Key Terms
تفويض الطلاق (tafwīd al-ṭalāq): Delegation by the husband to the wife of the power to divorce herself.
إيلاء (īlā'): An oath by the husband to abstain from marital relations for four months or more.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a wife in a delegated divorce says 'حرمت' without the intention of divorce?
A) One ba'in divorce
B) One raj'i divorce
C) It is an oath of ila'
D) No effect

Key Takeaway
Without intention, 'حرمت' is an oath of ila'; 'اخترت' alone cannot effect a divorce.

The Effect of 'Fa' vs 'Wa' in Tafwid: 'جعلت الخيار إلي'
Explains the effect of 'fa' vs 'wa' in delegated divorce when using 'جعلت الخيار إلي'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal consequences of using 'fa' versus 'wa' when a wife says 'جعلت الخيار إلي' in delegated divorce.

Core Idea
In tafwid, 'fa' makes the following phrase explanatory (tafsir), so divorce occurs immediately; 'wa' indicates a new beginning, resulting in a raj'i divorce with option.

This lesson focuses on a critical linguistic distinction: when a wife says 'I made the choice to me' followed by 'so I am divorced' (with fa) versus 'and I am divorced' (with wa). The conjunction determines whether the divorce is effective immediately or revocable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conjunction
Look at whether the wife used 'fa' (فاء) or 'wa' (واو) after the initial delegation phrase.
2
Analyze the function of 'fa'
The 'fa' is for explanation (tafsir), so the second statement explains the first; the divorce is based on the second phrase and is effective immediately.
3
Analyze the function of 'wa'
The 'wa' is for beginning (ibtida'), so it introduces a new sentence; the divorce is raj'i (revocable) and the wife has the option (takhyir).
4
Understand the role of takhyir vs ikhtiyar
Takhyir is the husband's act of giving choice; ikhtiyar is the wife's act of choosing. Only takhyir is legally significant in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

She is forbidden (to him) and without intention it is an oath of ila' because it is an oath. As for 'اخترت', it does not occur (divorce) because there is no literal meaning nor custom except as a response. Similarly, if she said 'I made the choice to me' or 'my affair is in my hand' and then said 'so I am divorced' (fa-tallaqt), because the فاء is for explanation, so the explained (mufassar) is considered and the (first) is void due to lack of prior tamlik. Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the legal effects of different wordings in delegated divorce (tafwid al-talaq). Key Terms
التخيير (al-takhyīr): The husband's act of giving the wife the choice to divorce herself; a form of delegation.
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyār): The wife's act of choosing divorce in response to takhyir.
التمليك (al-tamlīk): Legally granting ownership; in tafwid, prior tamlik is required for the divorce to take effect from the delegation.

Temporal Qualification and Third-Party Delegation in Tafwid
Explains temporal qualifiers and third-party delegation in tafwid al-talaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the effect of temporal qualifications (like 'yesterday') and third-party delegation (fuduli) in tafwid al-talaq according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Temporal qualifiers limit the option to that time; if the time has passed, no divorce can be effected. Third-party delegation is contingent on ratification in the same session.

Beyond the basic wordings, the timing and agency of delegation matter. This lesson covers two nuanced issues: when the wife references a past time ('أمس'), and when a third party (fuduli) acts without prior authorization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the temporal qualifier
Look for words like 'أمس' (yesterday) that set a specific time for the option.
2
Determine the time of ja'l
The time is for the making (ja'l), so the option existed only at that past time.
3
Apply the rule for temporal qualification
If the husband ratifies after that time, no divorce occurs because the time for the option has passed.
4
Analyze third-party delegation (fuduli)
When a third party delegates without prior authority, the delegation is suspended; it becomes effective only upon ratification in the same session.
Contrast with sale, which cannot be suspended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Intention of Three in Takhyir: Hanafi Position and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Ruling on intended three divorces in takhyir with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that intending three divorces in takhyir is invalid and explain why only one ba'in divorce occurs, citing the proof and the disagreement with Zayd ibn Thabit and Malik.

Core Idea
In takhyir, the word 'choose' only conveys one divorce; intention does not multiply it.

When a husband gives his wife the choice to divorce herself (takhyir), the words used are clear and unambiguous. The Hanafi school holds that the intention of three divorces has no effect; only one irrevocable divorce (ba'in) occurs. This is based on the linguistic clarity of the term 'ikhtiyar' and the consensus of the Companions. However, Zayd ibn Thabit and Imam Malik (in consummated marriages) argued that three divorces can occur by intention. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi proof and the practical consequence of this difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Determine what the Hanafi school rules regarding the intention of three divorces in takhyir. Note that this applies when the husband says 'اختاري' without specifying a number.
The ruling is that the intention is invalid; only one ba'in divorce falls.
2
Trace the Hanafi dalil
Examine the two proofs: (1) the word 'اختيار' linguistically implies purity and clarity, not multiplicity; (2) the consensus of the Companions that only one divorce occurs.
The consensus is cited as 'الأجماع منعقد على الواحدة'.
3
Understand the opposing position
Learn the view of Zayd ibn Thabit and Malik: they held that 'اختيار' can imply complete separation, so three divorces can be intended. Malik restricted this to consummated marriages.
Their proof is the possible interpretation of complete extraction (kull al-istikhlās).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the word is unambiguous because it is not varied and consensus supports one. The minority view is rejected.
The thamarah al-khilaf: one vs. three divorces in practice.
5
Apply to scenarios with numbers or repetition
If the husband says 'اختاري ثلاثاً' or repeats 'اختاري اختاري', the rulings change: explicit numbers or repeated commands lead to multiple divorces.
This is an extension of the same principle: the wording determines the number.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولم تصح فيه نية الثلاث لأنه إنما يفيد الخلوص والصفا فهو غير متنوع والبينونة ثبتت فيه مقتضى أنت بائن ونحوه لتنوع البينونة إلى غليظة وخفيفة. قيد بالاختيار لأن نية بخلاف الثلاث صحيحة في الأمر باليد كما سنذكره، وقول الشارحين إن الأجماع منعقد على الواحدة فبقي ما وراءه على الأصل منتف لأن زيد بن ثابت قال بوقوع الثلاث قولاً بكمال الاستخلاص وبه أخذ مالك في المدخول بها، وفي غيرها يقبل منه دعوى الواحدة، وسيأتي ما إذا جمع بين الأمر باليد والاختيار، وقيد بكون التخيير غير مقرون بعدد لأنه لو قال لها اختاري ثلاثاً فقالت اخترت يقع الثلاث لأن التنصيص على الثلاث دليل إرادة اختيار الطلاق الذي يتعدد وقولها اخترت ينصرف إليه فيقع الثلاث، فإن كرر التخيير بأن قال لها اختاري اختاري ونوى بكل واحدة منهما الطلاق فقالت اخترت يقع ثنتان لأن كل واحدة منهما تخيير تام بنفسه، وقولها اخترت جواباً لهما والواقع بكل منهما طلاق بائن، وكذا إذا ذكر الثاني بحرف الواو أو الفاء كذا في البدائع وسيأتي تمامه عند قوله «اخترت الأولى إلى آخره» قوله : (فإن قامت أو أخذت في عمل آخر بطل خيارها) لكونه تمليكاً فيبطل بتبدل المجلس حقيقة أو حكماً. أطلق القيام فشمل ما إذا أقامها الزوج قهراً فإنه يخرج الأمر من يدها لأنه يمكنها ممانعته من القيام أو المبادرة حينئذ إلى اختيارها نفسها فعدم ذلك دليل على الإعراض كما إذا جامعها مكرهة في مجلسها كما في الخلاصة. وأراد بالعمل الآخر ما يدل على الإعراض لا مطلق العمل لأنه لو خيرها فلبست ثوباً أو شربت لا يبطل خيارها لأن اللبس قد يكون لتدعو الشهود، والعطش قد يكون شديداً يمنع من التأمل، وسيأتي بيانه في فصل الأمر باليد فإن حكمه فيه كحكمه ودخل في العمل الكلام الأجنبي فإنه دليل الإعراض، وقيد بالاختيار لأن الصرف والسلم لا يبطلان بالإعراض بل بالافتراق لا عن قبض، والإيجاب في البيع يبطل بما يدل على الإعراض من القائل. وأفاد بعطفه الأخذ في العمل على القيام أنه يبطل بالقيام وإن لم يكن معه عمل آخر لأنه دليل الإعراض وهكذا بإطلاقه قول البعض، والأصح أنه يبطل به إلا إذا لم يشتمل على الإعراض وفائدة الاختلاف أنها لو قامت لتدعو شهوداً وتحولت من مكانها ولم يكن عندها أحد بطل خيارها عند البعض. قال في الخلاصة : والأصح أنه لا يبطل لعدم الإعراض، وأما إذا لم تتحول لا يبطل اتفاقاً. وقيد بكون التخيير مطلقاً لأنه لو كان موقتاً كما إذا قال اختاري نفسك اليوم أو هذا الشهر أو شهراً أو سنة فلها أن تختار ما دام الوقت باقياً، سواء أعرضت عن ذلك المجلس أو لا. كذا في الجوهرة، وسيأتي تمامه في فصل الأمر باليد قوله : (وذكر النفس أو الاختيارة في أحد كلاميهما شرط) فلو قال لها اختاري فقالت اخترت نفسي أو قال لها اختاري نفسك فقالت اخترت وقع، فإذا كانت النفس في كلاميهما فبالأولى، وإذا خلت عن كلاميهما لم يقع والاختيارة كالنفس وليس مراده خصوص النفس أو الإختيارة بل كل لفظ قام مقامهما يصلح تفسيراً للمبهم لأن الاختيار مبهم وإن كان ما وقع عليه إجماع الصحابة رضي الله عنهم
His saying: (And the intention of three does not become valid in it) because it only signifies purity and clarity, so it is not varied, and separation (baynuna) is established in it due to the implication of 'you are ba'in' and similar, due to the variation of baynuna into severe and light. He restricted to ikhtiyar because intention of three is valid in 'amr bil-yad' as we will mention. And the saying of the commentators that consensus is established on one, so what remains is negated by principle, because Zayd ibn Thabit said that three divorce occurs, a saying of complete extraction, and Malik adopted it for those who have been consummated with, and for others he accepts his claim of one. And later will come the case when one combines 'amr bil-yad' and takhyir. And he restricted that takhyir is not accompanied by a number because if he says 'choose three times' and she says 'I choose', three divorces occur because the specification of three indicates the desire to choose multiple divorce, and her saying 'I choose' refers to that, so three occur. If he repeated takhyir by saying 'choose, choose' and intended divorce in each, and she said 'I choose', two divorces occur because each is a complete takhyir in itself, and her saying 'I choose' is a response to both, and what falls is ba'in divorce from each. Similarly if the second is mentioned with waw or fa, as in al-Bada'i. And later its completion will come at his saying 'I choose the first' etc. His saying: (Then if she stands up or engages in another action, her option becomes void) because it is tamlik (delegation), so it becomes void by change of session, literally or figuratively. He generalised standing, so it includes if the husband forces her to stand, because then the affair leaves her hand since she can prevent him from standing or hasten to choose herself at that time, so not doing so is proof of disinterest, as if he coerced her in her session, as in al-Khulasa. And by 'another action' he means what indicates disinterest, not any action, because if he gave her choice and she put on a dress or drank, her option does not become void, because dressing may be to call witnesses, and thirst may be severe preventing deliberation. Its explanation will come in the chapter on 'amr bil-yad', for its ruling there is like its ruling here. And foreign speech is included in action because it indicates disinterest. He restricted to ikhtiyar because sarf and salam do not become void by disinterest, but by separation without receipt, and the ijaab in sale becomes void by what indicates disinterest from the speaker. And by conjoining 'taking in action' with 'standing', he indicated that it becomes void by standing even if not accompanied by another action, because it indicates disinterest; and thus, by its absoluteness, it is the statement of some. And the correct view is that it becomes void by it unless it does not contain disinterest. The benefit of the difference is that if she stood up to call witnesses and moved from her place and there was no one with her, her option becomes void according to some. In al-Khulasa: the correct view is that it does not become void due to lack of disinterest. And if she did not move, it does not become void by agreement. And he restricted that the takhyir is absolute, because if it is temporary, as if he said 'choose yourself today' or 'this month' or 'a month' or 'a year', then she may choose as long as the time remains, whether she turned away from that session or not. Thus in al-Jawhara. And its completion will come in the chapter on 'amr bil-yad'. His saying: (And the mention of 'nafs' or 'ikhtiyara' in one of their two statements is a condition). So if he said to her 'choose' and she said 'I choose myself', or he said 'choose yourself' and she said 'I choose', it falls. If the word nafs is in both, then a fortiori. If it is absent from both, it does not fall. And 'ikhtiyara' is like 'nafs'. He does not mean specifically nafs or ikhtiyara, rather every word that stands in their place and is suitable as an interpretation of the ambiguous, because the word 'ikhtiyar' is ambiguous, although what is established by consensus of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them.
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the discussion on delegation of divorce (tafwid). It begins by addressing the issue of intending three divorces in the context of takhyir (giving choice). The Hanafi position is that such intention is not valid because the word 'اختاري' (choose) implies only one clear divorce, not multiple. The commentator mentions that Zayd ibn Thabit and Imam Malik (in the case of a consummated marriage) held that three divorces can occur by intention, but the Hanafi school rejects this based on the principle that the wording is unambiguous and consensus among the Companions supports one divorce. The passage then discusses the effect of the wife standing up or engaging in other actions after being given the choice. The general rule is that standing or any action that indicates disinterest (i'rad) nullifies her option, because the delegation is considered a tamlik (transfer of ownership) which is tied to the session (majlis). However, not every action has this effect; actions that do not indicate disinterest, such as putting on a dress to call witnesses or drinking due to extreme thirst, do not nullify the option. There is a difference of opinion: some scholars hold that standing alone always nullifies the option, but the correct view (sahih) is that it only nullifies if it indicates disinterest. Similarly, if the option is restricted to a specific time period (e.g., 'choose today'), the wife may choose at any time within that period, even if she leaves the session. Finally, the passage stresses that for the divorce to be valid, the word 'nafs' (self) or 'ikhtiyara' (choice) must appear in at least one of the two statements (the husband's or the wife's). If neither statement contains such a word, the divorce does not fall, because the word 'ikhtiyar' (choice) is ambiguous. The commentator notes that any word serving the same purpose suffices.

Key Terms
التخيير (al-takhyīr): Giving the wife the choice to divorce herself, typically by saying 'اختاري'.
البائنونة (al-baynūna): Irrevocable separation (ba'in) that ends the marriage without right to return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the Hanafi ruling that intention of three is invalid in takhyir?
A) The word 'اختيار' is ambiguous.
B) Consensus of the Companions that only one divorce occurs.
C) The husband's intention is always disregarded.
D) The wife must explicitly state the number.

Key Takeaway
In delegated divorce with 'اختيار', only one ba'in divorce falls; the intention of three has no effect due to unambiguous wording and Companion consensus.

Effect of Standing and Actions on the Wife's Option in Delegated Divorce
Conditions for nullifying the wife's option in tafwid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when the wife's option in delegated divorce becomes void due to her actions, distinguishing between actions that indicate disinterest and those that do not.

Core Idea
The wife's option is voided only by actions that indicate disinterest, not by any movement or action.

When a husband gives his wife the right to divorce herself (tafwid), the option is tied to the session (majlis) and to her active interest. If she stands up or engages in another action that shows she is no longer considering the offer, her option lapses. However, not every movement nullifies the option; only actions that clearly demonstrate disinterest (i'rad) have this effect. This lesson unpacks the details, including the difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars on whether standing alone always voids the option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The general rule is that standing up or engaging in another action voids the wife's option because it is a tamlik (transfer of ownership) tied to the session.
The session change, physically or figuratively, ends the option.
2
Distinguish actions that indicate disinterest
Not all actions are equal; only those that signal disinterest (i'rad) void the option. Actions like putting on a dress to call witnesses or drinking due to thirst do not indicate disinterest.
The passage gives examples: لبس ثوب (dressing) and شرب (drinking) are not voiding if there is a legitimate reason.
3
Examine the ruling on standing alone
There is a difference of opinion: some say standing always voids the option, but the correct (sahih) view is that it voids only if it indicates disinterest. If the wife stands to call witnesses but does not move from her place, it does not void.
The benefit of the difference: if she stands and moves to another place, some say it voids, but the correct view says no if no disinterest.
4
Include foreign speech and temporary options
Foreign speech unrelated to the option also indicates disinterest and voids. However, if the option is time-bound (e.g., 'choose today'), she may choose at any time within that period, even if she leaves the session.
Temporary options are an exception to the session requirement.

باليد كما سنذكره، وقول الشارحين إن الأجماع منعقد على الواحدة فبقي ما وراءه على الأصل منتف لأن زيد بن ثابت قال بوقوع الثلاث قولاً بكمال الاستخلاص وبه أخذ مالك في المدخول بها، وفي غيرها يقبل منه دعوى الواحدة، وسيأتي ما إذا جمع بين الأمر باليد والاختيار، وقيد بكون التخيير غير مقرون بعدد لأنه لو قال لها اختاري ثلاثاً فقالت اخترت يقع الثلاث لأن التنصيص على الثلاث دليل إرادة اختيار الطلاق الذي يتعدد المجلس حقيقة أو حكماً. أطلق القيام فشمل ما إذا أقامها الزوج قهراً فإنه يخرج الأمر من يدها لأنه يمكنها ممانعته من القيام أو المبادرة حينئذ إلى اختيارها نفسها فعدم ذلك دليل على الإعراض كما إذا جامعها His saying: (And the intention of three does not become valid in it) because it only signifies purity and clarity, so it is not varied, and separation (baynuna) is established in it due to the implication of 'you are ba'in' and similar, due to the variation of baynuna into severe and light. الإعراض (al-iʿrāḍ): Disinterest or turning away from the option, evidenced by actions like standing or engaging in unrelated tasks.
المجلس (al-majlis): The session in which the delegation and acceptance occur; a change of session can nullify the option.

Condition of Mentioning 'Nafs' or 'Ikhtiyara' in Delegated Divorce
Condition for valid divorce in tafwid: mention of 'nafs' or 'ikhtiyara'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition that the word 'nafs' (self) or 'ikhtiyara' (choice) must appear in the statement of either the husband or wife for the divorce to be valid in tafwid.

Core Idea
The word 'ikhtiyar' is ambiguous; for a valid divorce, 'nafs' or 'ikhtiyara' must clarify it.

In delegated divorce (tafwid), the husband's command 'اختاري' (choose) is ambiguous by itself; it does not necessarily refer to divorce. To remove this ambiguity, either the husband or the wife must use a word that clarifies the intention, such as 'نفسك' (yourself) or 'اختيارة' (choice). This ensures that the divorce is valid and not left to interpretation. The Hanafi school considers this condition based on the consensus of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the ambiguity of 'ikhtiyar'
The word 'اختيار' (choice) is ambiguous; it could mean choosing anything, not necessarily divorce. Therefore, it needs clarification.
The passage says 'الاختيار مبهم'.
2
State the condition
The condition is that the word 'نفس' (self) or 'اختيارة' (choice) must appear in the statement of either the husband or the wife. If present in both, a fortiori valid.
Examples: if he says 'اختاري نفسك' and she says 'اخترت', or he says 'اختاري' and she says 'اخترت نفسي'.
3
Apply the condition to examples
If the word 'نفس' or 'اختيارة' is missing from both statements, the divorce does not fall. If it is present in one, it is valid.
The passage gives the example: 'فلو قال لها اختاري فقالت اخترت نفسي' (valid), and 'لو قال لها اختاري نفسك فقالت اخترت' (valid).
4
Generalise the ruling
Any word that stands in place of 'نفس' or 'اختيارة' and is suitable as an interpretation of the ambiguous term suffices.
The condition is not limited to these exact words; any clarifying expression works.

بكون التخيير مطلقاً لأنه لو كان موقتاً كما إذا قال اختاري نفسك اليوم أو هذا الشهر أو شهراً أو سنة فلها أن تختار ما دام الوقت باقياً، سواء أعرضت عن ذلك المجلس أو لا. كذا في الجوهرة، وسيأتي تمامه في فصل الأمر باليد قوله : (وذكر النفس أو الاختيارة في أحد كلاميهما شرط) فلو قال لها اختاري فقالت اخترت نفسي أو قال لها اختاري نفسك فقالت اخترت وقع، فإذا كانت النفس في كلاميهما فبالأولى، وإذا خلت عن كلاميهما لم يقع والاختيارة كالنفس وليس مراده خصوص النفس أو الإختيارة بل كل لفظ قام مقامهما يصلح تفسيراً للمبهم لأن الاختيار مبهم وإن كان ما وقع عليه إجماع الصحابة رضي الله عنهم
His saying: (And the intention of three does not become valid in it) because it only signifies purity and clarity, so it is not varied, and separation (baynuna) is established in it due to the implication of 'you are ba'in' and similar, due to the variation of baynuna into severe and light. He restricted to ikhtiyar because intention of three is valid in 'amr bil-yad' as we will mention. And the saying of the commentators that consensus is established on one, so what remains is negated by principle, because Zayd ibn Thabit said that three divorce occurs, a saying of complete extraction, and Malik adopted it for those who have been consummated with, and for others he accepts his claim of one. And later will come the case when one combines 'amr bil-yad' and takhyir. And he restricted that takhyir is not accompanied by a number because if he says 'choose three times' and she says 'I choose', three divorces occur because the specification of three indicates the desire to choose multiple divorce, and her saying 'I choose' refers to that, so three occur. If he repeated takhyir by saying 'choose, choose' and intended divorce in each, and she said 'I choose', two divorces occur because each is a complete takhyir in itself, and her saying 'I choose' is a response to both, and what falls is ba'in divorce from each. Similarly if the second is mentioned with waw or fa, as in al-Bada'i. And later its completion will come at his saying 'I choose the first' etc. His saying: (Then if she stands up or engages in another action, her option becomes void) because it is tamlik (delegation), so it becomes void by change of session, literally or figuratively. He generalised standing, so it includes if the husband forces her to stand, because then the affair leaves her hand since she can prevent him from standing or hasten to choose herself at that time, so not doing so is proof of disinterest, as if he coerced her in her session, as in al-Khulasa. And by 'another action' he means what indicates disinterest, not any action, because if he gave her choice and she put on a dress or drank, her option does not become void, because dressing may be to call witnesses, and thirst may be severe preventing deliberation. Its explanation will come in the chapter on 'amr bil-yad', for its ruling there is like its ruling here. And foreign speech is included in action because it indicates disinterest. He restricted to ikhtiyar because sarf and salam do not become void by disinterest, but by separation without receipt, and the ijaab in sale becomes void by what indicates disinterest from the speaker. And by conjoining 'taking in action' with 'standing', he indicated that it becomes void by standing even if not accompanied by another action, because it indicates disinterest; and thus, by its absoluteness, it is the statement of some. And the correct view is that it becomes void by it unless it does not contain disinterest. The benefit of the difference is that if she stood up to call witnesses and moved from her place and there was no one with her, her option becomes void according to some. In al-Khulasa: the correct view is that it does not become void due to lack of disinterest. And if she did not move, it does not become void by agreement. And he restricted that the takhyir is absolute, because if it is temporary, as if he said 'choose yourself today' or 'this month' or 'a month' or 'a year', then she may choose as long as the time remains, whether she turned away from that session or not. Thus in al-Jawhara. And its completion will come in the chapter on 'amr bil-yad'. His saying: (And the mention of 'nafs' or 'ikhtiyara' in one of their two statements is a condition). So if he said to her 'choose' and she said 'I choose myself', or he said 'choose yourself' and she said 'I choose', it falls. If the word nafs is in both, then a fortiori. If it is absent from both, it does not fall. And 'ikhtiyara' is like 'nafs'. He does not mean specifically nafs or ikhtiyara, rather every word that stands in their place and is suitable as an interpretation of the ambiguous, because the word 'ikhtiyar' is ambiguous, although what is established by consensus of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them.
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the discussion on delegation of divorce (tafwid). It begins by addressing the issue of intending three divorces in the context of takhyir (giving choice). The Hanafi position is that such intention is not valid because the word 'اختاري' (choose) implies only one clear divorce, not multiple. The commentator mentions that Zayd ibn Thabit and Imam Malik (in the case of a consummated marriage) held that three divorces can occur by intention, but the Hanafi school rejects this based on the principle that the wording is unambiguous and consensus among the Companions supports one divorce. The passage then discusses the effect of the wife standing up or engaging in other actions after being given the choice. The general rule is that standing or any action that indicates disinterest (i'rad) nullifies her option, because the delegation is considered a tamlik (transfer of ownership) which is tied to the session (majlis). However, not every action has this effect; actions that do not indicate disinterest, such as putting on a dress to call witnesses or drinking due to extreme thirst, do not nullify the option. There is a difference of opinion: some scholars hold that standing alone always nullifies the option, but the correct view (sahih) is that it only nullifies if it indicates disinterest. Similarly, if the option is restricted to a specific time period (e.g., 'choose today'), the wife may choose at any time within that period, even if she leaves the session. Finally, the passage stresses that for the divorce to be valid, the word 'nafs' (self) or 'ikhtiyara' (choice) must appear in at least one of the two statements (the husband's or the wife's). If neither statement contains such a word, the divorce does not fall, because the word 'ikhtiyar' (choice) is ambiguous. The commentator notes that any word serving the same purpose suffices.

Key Terms
النفس (al-nafs): Self; a word that clarifies the ambiguous 'ikhtiyar'.
الاختيارة (al-ikhtiyāra): Choice; a word that serves the same clarifying function as 'nafs'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the wife says 'اخترت' without any mention of 'نفس' after the husband says 'اختاري'?
A) The divorce is valid because she accepted.
B) The divorce is invalid because the wording is ambiguous.
C) It depends on the husband's intention.
D) The wife must repeat with 'نفس'.

Key Takeaway
In delegated divorce, the word 'نفس' or 'اختيارة' (or equivalent) must appear in at least one of the statements to remove ambiguity and validate the divorce.

Talaq — Divorce

Invalidation of Wife's Option and Requirement of Verbal Interpreter in Delegated Divorce
Commentary on matn rulings regarding delegation of divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which the wife's option in delegated divorce is invalidated and apply the Hanafi rule that a verbal interpreter (nafs or ikhtiyara) is required for the divorce to occur.

Core Idea
In Hanafi delegated divorce, the wife's option is invalidated by actions indicating disinterest, and the divorce only occurs if she explicitly mentions 'myself' or 'choice'.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains two critical rulings in delegated divorce (tafwīḍ al-ṭalāq). First, when the husband gives his wife the choice to divorce herself, her right (khiyār) is treated as ownership and is lost if she stands or engages in another action showing disinterest. Second, for the divorce to take effect, the wife must verbally interpret the ambiguous word 'choose' by saying 'I choose myself' or similar. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying delegated divorce in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of delegation
Recognize that tafwīḍ al-ṭalāq is a tamlīk (ownership) of the right to divorce. The wife's khiyār is analogous to an option in a sale contract, which is voided by a change of assembly (majlis) or actions indicating disinterest (i'rāḍ).
The passage states 'li-annahu tamlīkan fa-yabṭulu bi-tabadduli al-majlisi ḥaqīqatan aw ḥukman'.
2
Identify actions that invalidate the option
Distinguish between actions that indicate disinterest (standing up, starting another work) and neutral actions (drinking, dressing due to necessity). Only the former invalidate the option. The sounder view (al-aṣaḥḥ) is that standing alone invalidates only if it indicates disinterest.
The text says 'wa'ashah annahu lā yabṭulu illā idhā lam yashtamil 'alā al-i'rāḍ'.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Some Hanafis hold that any standing invalidates the option regardless of intent; the sounder view is that it must indicate disinterest. This affects cases like standing to call witnesses.
Refer to the debate in al-Khulāṣa and al-Jawhara.
4
Recognize the requirement of a verbal interpreter
The word 'choose' (ikhtārī) is ambiguous (mubham). For divorce to occur, the wife must verbally interpret it by mentioning 'nafs' (self) or 'ikhtiyāra' (choice) in one of the utterances. Intention alone or contextual clues are insufficient.
The passage cites Ijmā' al-Ṣaḥāba as proof that a verbal interpreter is needed.
5
Contrast with other schools
Shafi'i and Hanbali schools accept intention with circumstantial evidence (qarīna), but Hanafis insist on a clear verbal statement based on the consensus of the Companions. The practical difference: Hanafis require explicit mention of 'nafs'; others may validate without it.
The passage explicitly rejects the opposing view as bāṭil.

Source Text

كلامه إنما هو بالنفس لأنه عرف من إجماعهم اعتبار مفسر لفظاً من جانب فيقتصر عليه فينتفي غير المفسر. وأما خصوص لفظ المفسر فمعلوم الإلغاء فدخل فيه ذكر التطليقة. وتكرار قوله اختاري وقولها اختار أبي أو أمي Book of Divorce, Chapter of Delegation of Divorce. The three: if she stands or starts another action, her option is invalidated. And mentioning the self or the choice in one of their two statements... [full translation omitted due to length; matches Arabic]
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, covering the chapter on delegation of divorce (tafwid al-talaq). It addresses two main rulings: (1) When the wife's option to choose herself (khayar) is invalidated by her actions, and (2) the requirement that the word 'self' (nafs) or 'choice' (ikhtiyara) appear in the utterances for divorce to take effect.

The first part explains that if the husband gives his wife the choice (e.g., 'Choose yourself'), her option is ownership (tamlik) and is therefore cancelled by a change of assembly (majlis), either literally or constructively. Standing up or engaging in another action indicates disinterest (i'rad), thus voiding the option. However, not all actions void it—only those that show disinterest. For example, if she puts on a garment or drinks due to necessity, the option remains. There is a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars: some hold that standing alone invalidates the option even without another action, while the sounder view (al-ashah) is that it invalidates unless it does not indicate disinterest. The ruling is restricted to unconditional delegation; if it is time-bound (e.g., 'choose today'), the option lasts for the duration.

The second part discusses the condition that the word 'self' (nafs) or 'choice' (ikhtiyara) must appear in one of the two statements. If the husband says 'Choose' and she says 'I choose myself', divorce occurs. If she says 'I choose' without 'myself', it does not occur because the choice is ambiguous and requires a verbal interpreter (mufassir) from one side. The author mentions various scenarios: if she says 'I choose my father/mother/relatives' or 'my husband' (if she had a previous husband), divorce may or may not occur depending on interpretation. The discussion includes a disagreement with the Shafi'i and Hanbali schools, who accept intention (niyya) with circumstantial evidence, but the Hanafi school insists on a verbal interpreter. The author notes a conflict with the view of Taj al-Shari'a regarding mutual confirmation after the session, and the sounder view is that mutual confirmation after the session is not sufficient—the verbal interpreter must be present in the session. The passage also addresses the repetition of the word 'choose' as an indicator of intent for divorce, but this is only for those who do not require intention; for those who require it, repetition does not suffice.

The author concludes with a note that the dispute with Shafi'i and Ahmad is invalid because the word 'choose' cannot be used for divorce without a verbal interpreter, and intention alone is insufficient.

Key Terms
تفويض الطلاق (tafwīḍ al-ṭalāq): Delegation of divorce: the husband gives the wife the authority to divorce herself by her own choice.
خيار (khiyār): Option: the wife's right to choose herself (i.e., to divorce) within the council or specified period.
النفس (al-nafs): The self: used as a verbal interpreter in the wife's response to indicate she chooses herself.
اختيارة (ikhtiyāra): The choice: synonymous with 'nafs' as a verbal interpreter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what happens if the husband says 'Ikhtari' (choose) to his wife and she responds 'Ikhtartu' (I choose) without mentioning herself?
A) Divorce occurs because the choice is clear.
B) Divorce does not occur because the wife did not mention 'nafs' (self).
C) Divorce occurs only if the husband intended it.
D) Divorce is suspended pending the husband's confirmation.

Key Takeaway
In Ḥanafī delegated divorce, the wife's option is invalidated only by actions indicating disinterest, and the divorce is not valid unless she explicitly mentions 'myself' or 'choice' as a verbal interpreter.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Wife's Explicit Choice and Customary Exceptions in Delegated Divorce
Core conditions for wife's response to delegated divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a wife's response to 'Ikhari' effects divorce and distinguish between explicit self-choice and other customary choices.

Core Idea
For delegated divorce, the wife must explicitly choose herself; other choices only work by custom.

In this lesson, we examine the core ruling that the wife's response to the husband's delegation 'Ikhari' must explicitly mention herself (nafsi) for divorce to occur. We also explore exceptions where custom allows her choice of a brother or family members to be effective, and note the linguistic authority of Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous utterance
The husband's 'Ikhari' is ambiguous; it could mean choose your husband, family, etc. Therefore, it requires a clarifier (mufassir) from the wife to specify the intent of divorce.
The Hanafi school insists on a verbal clarifier because the word 'ikhtari' is not inherently indicative of divorce.
2
Recognize the required clarifier
The wife must explicitly mention herself ('nafsi') in her response, e.g., 'I choose myself' or 'I chose myself.' This is the default condition for divorce to take effect.
Mentioning anyone else (father, mother, husband) does not effect divorce unless a custom-based exception applies.
3
Understand the default negation
If the wife chooses her father, mother, family, or a mahram relative, divorce does not occur. The text explicitly states that such choices do not constitute a valid divorce.
This default is based on the principle that the ambiguous word 'ikhtari' cannot be clarified by non-self references without specific textual allowance.
4
Apply the customary exception
When the wife has no living parents and she has a brother, choosing her brother does effect divorce by custom (urf). This is reported from Fath al-Qadir and accepted in the Hanafi school.
The exception is based on the practical custom that a woman without parents often seeks separation through her brother.
5
Note the linguistic authority of Imam Muhammad
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, known for his linguistic expertise, defined 'ahl' as parents and 'qawm' as other relatives. His definitions are authoritative in the Hanafi school for interpreting these terms in divorce contexts.
This linguistic distinction affects the ruling: choosing 'ahl' (parents) is not effective, but choosing 'qawm' (other relatives) may be effective under custom.

كتاب الطلاق باب تفويض الطلاق
كلامه إنما هو بالنفس لأنه عرف من إجماعهم اعتبار مفسر لفظاً من جانب فيقتصر عليه فينتفي غير المفسر. وأما خصوص لفظ المفسر فمعلوم الإلغاء فدخل فيه ذكر التطليقة. وتكرار قوله اختاري وقولها اختار أبي أو أمي أو أهلي أو الأزواج بخلاف اخترت قومي أو ذا رحم محرم فإنه لا يقع، وينبغي أن يحمل على ما إذا كان لها أب أو أم، أما إذا لم يكن لها ولها أخ فقالت اخترت أخي ينبغي أن يقع لأنها تكون عنده عادة عند البينونة إذا عدمت الوالدين كما في فتح القدير. وفي المحيط : لو قال اختاري أهلك أو الأزواج فاختارتهم وقع استحساناً، وكذا أباك وأمك أو زوجك وهو محمول على ما إذا كان لها زوج قبله فخيرها فيه . ولو قال اختاري قومك أو ذا رحم محرم منك لا يقع، وإن اختارت نفسها فقد جعل محمد الأهل اسماً للأبوين والقوم اسماً لسائر الأقارب، وقوله حجة في اللغة لأنه من أرباب اللغة ا هـ . وحاصله أن المفسر من أحد الجانبين ثمانية ألفاظ كما قررناه، وقدمنا أن العدد في مفسر فهي تسع. وأشار بقوله في أحد كلاميهما إلى أنه لا بد في نية المفسر من الاتصال، فلو كان منفصلاً فإن كان في المجلس صح وإلا فلا. ولذا قال في المحيط والخانية : لو قالت في المجلس عنيت نفسي يقع لأنها ما دامت في المجلس تملك الإنشاء. وفي الفوائد التاجية : هذا إذا لم يصدقها الزوج أنها اختارت نفسها، فإن صدقها وقع الطلاق بتصادقهما وإن خلا كلامهما عن ذكر النفس ا هـ . وظاهر أن التصادق بعد المجلس معتبر. وفي فتح القدير : الإيقاع بالاختيار على خلاف القياس فيقتصر على مورد النص فيه، ولولا هذا لأمكن الاكتفاء بتفسير القرينة الحالية دون المقالية بعد أن نوى الزوج وقوع الطلاق به وتصادقا عليه لكنه باطل وإلا لوقع بمجرد النية . لفظ لا يصلح له أصلاً كاسقني ، ، وبهذا بطل اكتفاء الشافعي وأحمد بالنية مع القرينة عند ذكر النفس ونحوه ا هـ . وهذا مخالف لما ذكرناه عن تاج الشريعة الاكتفاء بالتصادق فليتأمل قوله : (ولو قال لها اختاري فقالت أنا أختار نفسي أو اخترت نفسي تطلق) لوجود الشرط أي تبين وإنما ذكر الثانية وهي قولها «اخترت نفسي» وإن كان من قوله: (وتكرار لفظ اختاري كون التكرار مفسراً لإرادة الطلاق مبني على قول من لم يشترط النية، أما من اشترطها لا يجعل التكرار مفسراً للمراد فيلزمه أن لا يكتفي به عن ذكر النفس وإلا لزم استعمال لفظ الاختيار مبهماً بلا مفسر لفظي وهو خلاف الإجماع وسنذكر تمام تحقيقه. فتدبر قوله : (وهذا مخالف لما ذكرناه عن تاج الشريعة قال الرملي قال في النهر وذكر في العناية ما ذكره في التاجية بقيل وفيه إيماء إلى ضعفه وهو الحق ا هـ . وبهذا يندفع ما في شرح المقدسي حيث قال : وأنت خبير بأنه إذا صدقها بعد المجلس على أنها نوت نفسها في المجلس كان اللفظ صالحاً للإيقاع فيحمل كلام الكمال على غير ذلك بأن تصادقا على الطلاق مع الإطلاق فتأمل قوله : (يشير إلى أن لفظ أنا الخ) انظر ما المعلل بهذا التعليل .
He said: It is only with the self because it is known from their consensus that a verbal clarifier from one side is taken into account, so it is limited to it, and non-clarified is negated. And as for the specific wording of the clarifier, it is known to be canceled, so the mention of 'one divorce' is included. And repeating his saying 'Ikhari' and her saying 'I choose my father or mother or family or husbands' is different from 'I chose my tribe or a mahram relative,' which does not occur. It should be understood as when she has a father or mother; if she has neither and she has a brother and she says 'I chose my brother,' it should occur because it is her custom at separation when parents are absent, as in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Muhit: If he said 'Choose your family or husbands' and she chose them, it occurs by istihsan, likewise 'your father and mother or your husband,' and it is understood as when she had a previous husband and he gave her choice. If he said 'Choose your tribe or a mahram relative of yours,' it does not occur; and if she chooses herself, then Muhammad made 'ahl' a name for parents and 'qawm' a name for other relatives, and his statement is authoritative in language because he is among linguists. The summary is that the clarifier from one side is eight words as we stated, and we previously said the number in 'mufassar' is nine. His indication in 'in one of his two statements' is that connection is necessary in the intention of the clarifier; if it is separate, then if it is in the session it is valid, otherwise not. Hence in al-Muhit and al-Khaniyyah: If she said in the session 'I meant myself,' it occurs because as long as she is in the session she has the power of initiation. In al-Fawa'id al-Tajiyyah: This is if the husband does not believe her that she chose herself; if he believes her, divorce occurs by their mutual confirmation even if their speech lacks mention of self. It is apparent that confirmation after the session is valid. In Fath al-Qadir: Effecting divorce by choice is contrary to analogy, so it is limited to the text. Were it not for this, it would be possible to suffice with interpretation by contextual evidence without verbal interpretation after the husband intended divorce and they agree, but it is invalid, otherwise it would occur by mere intention. The word is not suitable for it originally, like 'give me water.' And with this, the sufficiency of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad by intention with contextual evidence when mentioning self etc. is invalid. This contradicts what we mentioned from Taj al-Shari'ah sufficing with mutual confirmation, so it should be considered. His saying: (If he said to her 'Ikhari' and she said 'I choose myself' or 'I chose myself,' she is divorced) due to existence of condition, i.e., she becomes separate. He mentioned the second, 'I chose myself' although it is from his saying: (And repetition of the word 'Ikhari' is a clarifier for intending divorce based on the view of those who do not require intention. As for those who require it, repetition is not a clarifier for the intended, so he must not suffice with it instead of mentioning self, otherwise the word 'choice' would be used vaguely without a verbal clarifier, which is against consensus. We will mention its full investigation. So ponder on his saying: (This contradicts what we mentioned from Taj al-Shari'ah: al-Ramli said in al-Nahr, and al-Inayah mentioned what was said in al-Tajiyyah with 'qil,' indicating its weakness, and it is the truth. With this, what is in Sharh al-Maqdisi is repelled, where he said: You are aware that if he believes her after the session that she intended herself in the session, the wording would be suitable for effecting divorce, so Kamal's speech is interpreted otherwise, that they agreed on divorce with absolute wording. So ponder his saying: (Indicating that the wording 'ana' etc.) Look at what is reasoned with this reasoning.
Source pages p. 544

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary on the delegation of divorce (tafwid al-talaq). The central ruling is that when a husband says to his wife 'Ikhari' (choose), her divorce is effected only if she explicitly mentions herself (nafsi) in her response, such as 'I choose myself' or 'I chose myself.' This is because the mere word 'Ikhari' is ambiguous; it could mean choose your husband, your family, etc. The Hanafi position requires a verbal clarifier (mufassir) from one side (the wife) to specify the intent of divorce. The passage discusses various scenarios: if she instead chooses her father, mother, or husband, divorce does not occur unless she has no parents and chooses her brother, in which case it occurs due to custom. If the husband specifies the options (e.g., 'choose your family or husbands') and she chooses them, divorce occurs by istihsan. The text also distinguishes between 'ahl' (parents) and 'qawm' (other relatives) based on Imam Muhammad's linguistic authority. Important: the intention (niyyah) must be connected to the utterance; if the wife clarifies her intention in the same session, it is valid. There is a dispute about whether mutual confirmation (tasadiq) after the session suffices—some say yes, others no. The passage also critiques the Shafi'i/Hanbali position of sufficing with intention and contextual evidence without an explicit verbal clarifier. The story of Aisha (RA) is referenced to support that the present tense 'ana akhtaru' indicates immediate effect, just as she responded 'Akhtartu Allah wa Rasulahu' and the Prophet accepted it as an exercise of choice.

Key Terms
تفويض الطلاق (tafwid al-talaq): The husband delegating the decision of divorce to his wife by saying 'Ikhari' (choose) or similar.
مفسر (mufassir): A verbal clarifier that specifies the intended meaning of an ambiguous word; here, the wife's mention of 'nafsi' clarifies that she intends divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'Ikhari' and the wife responds 'I chose my brother' and she has no living parents, what is the ruling?
A) Divorce does not occur.
B) Divorce occurs because the brother is akin to parents.
C) Divorce occurs by custom.
D) It is a valid choice but not divorce.

Key Takeaway
In delegated divorce, the wife's explicit mention of herself is required for divorce; however, customary exceptions (like choosing a brother when parents are absent) are recognized.

Talaq — Divorce

10 min
Tasadiq After the Session and Hanafi Rejection of Contextual Evidence
Intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab disputes on the necessity of verbal clarifier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on mutual confirmation after the session and critique the Shafi'i position of sufficing with intention and contextual evidence.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school requires a verbal clarifier in the session; post-session confirmation is debated, and intention alone is insufficient.

This lesson focuses on the latter part of the passage, which discusses the necessity of connection in intention, the possibility of mutual confirmation (tasadiq) after the session, and the rejection of the Shafi'i/Hanbali view that intention with contextual evidence suffices without an explicit verbal clarifier.

Guided Path
1
Identify the requirement for connection
The passage states that the intention (niyyah) of the clarifier must be connected to the utterance. If the wife clarifies her intention in the same session, it is valid; if after the session, it is not, according to the main position.
Connection here means the clarification must occur while the wife is still in the same gathering (majlis) as the husband's delegation.
2
Understand the tasadiq option
If the husband confirms (tasadiq) that she intended herself, divorce occurs even without her verbal mention of self, according to some Hanafi scholars like Taj al-Shari'ah.
This view is found in al-Fawa'id al-Tajiyyah and is based on the idea that mutual confirmation establishes the intent.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
There is a disagreement within the Hanafi school: Taj al-Shari'ah holds that tasadiq after the session is sufficient, while Fath al-Qadir and the author of this passage (Ibn Nujaym) argue that it is not, because the original act of delegation requires a verbal clarifier in the session.
The author indicates that the view of Taj al-Shari'ah is weak (qil), and the relied-upon position is that post-session tasadiq is not valid.
4
Examine the cross-madhhab critique
The passage explicitly critiques the Shafi'i and Hanbali schools for sufficing with intention and contextual evidence (qarinah haliyyah) without a verbal clarifier. The Hanafi argument is that the word 'Ikhari' is not inherently suitable for initiating divorce; therefore, an explicit verbal clarifier is necessary.
The passage says: 'Were it not for this, it would be possible to suffice with interpretation by contextual evidence... but it is invalid, otherwise it would occur by mere intention.'
5
Note the author's stance and tarjih
The author sides with Fath al-Qadir against Taj al-Shari'ah, indicating that the mufta bihi position is that verbal clarification in the session is necessary and that post-session tasadiq is not sufficient.
This is the majority Hanafi position.

Source Text
