Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Wife's Divorce and 'Najawtu'
Refining exceptions to the principle of valid responses in delegated divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on whether the husband's saying 'najawtu' prevents divorce, and apply the rule that kinayat require intention.

Core Idea
Not all expressions valid from the husband are valid from the wife; attribution and intention are decisive.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq critically refines the general principle that any expression valid for the husband's divorce is also valid as a response from the wife when she has been delegated the right. It introduces exceptions from Al-Khulasa, particularly the case where the husband says 'najawtu' after the wife divorces herself, leading to an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying the rules of talaq in delegated divorce scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle and Its Exception
State the principle that expressions valid from the husband are valid from the wife. Then note the exception: some phrases like 'Anti minni taliq' are not valid from the wife because they misattribute the divorce.
The exception is based on the direction of attribution, not the literal wording.
2
Analyze the 'Najawtu' Case
Consider the scenario: husband says 'Your command is in your hand' (no number intended), wife says 'I divorced myself three times', husband responds 'I am saved' (najawtu). Two positions: Abu Hanifa says no divorce; the two companions say one divorce occurs.
The difference hinges on whether the husband's response indicates mockery or denial of delegation.
3
Understand the Role of Kinayah and Attribution
Examine why 'Anti minni taliq' fails: it attributes divorce to the husband ('you are divorced from me'), whereas the wife should attribute to herself ('I am divorced from you'). Similarly, 'I joined my family' is a kinayah requiring intention.
The Hanafi classification of kinayat determines whether they are effective without intention in the context of divorce discussion.
4
Apply the Principle that Kinayat for Rejection Require Intention
Phrases like 'alhaqtu nafsi bi ahli' are not automatically divorce; intention is needed. Contrast with 'anti haram' which is effective without intention during marriage discussion.
This distinction is based on the nature of the expression as indicating separation or rejection.
5
Evaluate the Intra-Hanafi Dispute and Thamarah
Practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, marriage continues; under the companions, one divorce occurs. The text leaves the issue unresolved but suggests the companions' view may be stronger.
The thamarah al-khilaf directly affects the marital status.

But what is in Al-Khulasa refutes the mentioned principle: If he placed her command in the hand of her father and her father said 'I accept', she is divorced. Likewise, if he placed her command in her hand and she said 'I accepted myself' and intended divorce but not a number, and she said 'I divorced myself three times', and the husband said 'I am saved', nothing occurs according to Imam Abu Hanifa because when he did not intend three, it is as if he said to her 'Divorce yourself' and did not intend a number. And his saying 'I am saved' may be interpreted as mockery, and one (divorce) occurs according to his two companions. End quote. But the author will mention in the chapter on will (mashi'a) at his statement 'no in its opposite' after quoting the mentioned branch that it is problematic according to what is in Al-Mabsut in the issue of al-amr bi al-yad, for it is transmitted that if he said to her 'Your command is in your hand' intending one, and she divorced three times, one occurs according to him, and it is mentioned in Al-Mi'raj and Al-Inaya. So if he said 'Your command is in your hand' and did not intend any number, and she divorced three times, how does one not occur according to him? Rather, occurrence is more appropriate. End quote. His statement: (And in Al-Khulasa if she said in response 'I owned my command' in some copies 'I owned myself my command' with an additional 'myself', and I did not find it in Al-Khulasa. His statement: 'But it refutes the mentioned principle' etc.) This is an objection to its opposite, which is his statement 'and what is not, is not'. And it is objected to regarding the exception like 'You are irrevocably separated from me' (anti minni taliq) because it is suitable for effecting divorce from him, yet it does not occur if she responds with it, as the author mentioned. And it may be answered regarding the second that it is not suitable for effecting divorce from him because her saying 'You are irrevocably separated from me' is a kinayah of her saying 'My husband Zayd is irrevocably separated from me', so its counterpart is 'I am irrevocably separated from you', not 'You are irrevocably separated from me'. And thus it does not occur because it is a kinayah of his saying 'Your husband Zayd is irrevocably separated from you'. And similarly, in its analogues, in her saying 'You are forbidden to me' and similar, it occurs because if he said 'Your husband Zayd is forbidden to you' or 'I am forbidden to you', it occurs because her saying 'you' is a kinayah for the apparent. Likewise, if she said 'I divorced myself', it occurs because her saying 'myself' is an expression for Zaynab, for example, and if he said 'I divorced Zaynab', it occurs. And likewise her saying 'I am irrevocably separated from you' or 'I am divorced' occurs because if he attributed divorce to what he is divorced by her saying 'I', it occurs, unlike 'You are divorced' because he attributed it to what he is divorced by, it does not occur as we said. So it is not from me. The intent is not the expression she used, but the attribution of divorce to whom she attributed it; otherwise it would not occur in her saying 'I am irrevocably separated from you'. His statement: (And it is problematic because it is from kinayat...) I say: In the expression of Jami' al-Fusulayn, there is what removes the difficulty. Its text: He said to his wife 'Divorce yourself', and she said 'I am forbidden' or 'free' or 'innocent' or 'irrevocably separated' or 'definitively' or similar; the principle is that everything that is divorce from the husband if she asks him and he responds with it, then if she effects the like upon herself after the divorce is in her hand, she is divorced. So if she said 'Divorce me' and he said 'You are forbidden' or 'irrevocably separated', she is divorced; so if he said it after the divorce is in her hand, she is also divorced. So if she said to him 'Divorce me' and he said 'Join your family' and said 'I did not intend divorce', he is believed and she is not divorced; so if she said it after the divorce is in her hand, such as she said 'I joined my family', she is not divorced also! End quote. And its explanation is that 'I joined my family' is from the kinayat that are suitable for rejection, so divorce does not occur by it except with intention, even in a state of anger or discussion of divorce, unlike 'forbidden', 'irrevocably separated', etc., for they occur in the context of discussion without intention. So if she asks him for divorce and he says 'You are forbidden', it occurs without intention, so if she says it, it also occurs, unlike 'Join your family', for it is not determined for effecting divorce after her asking except with intention, so if he says it, it does not occur. This is what appeared to me, so reflect. His statement: 'He is asked about intention' meaning if there is no situational indication. And therefore al-Maqdisi said after mentioning what was mentioned that it is necessary...

Commentary
This commentary passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides a critical refinement of the earlier principle that every expression valid for a husband's divorce is also valid as a response from the wife when she has been given the right to divorce. The author raises objections from Al-Khulasa. First, a case where the husband says to the wife's father 'I place her command in your hand', and the father says 'I accept'—the wife is divorced. But this seems to contradict the principle because 'accept' is not a divorce expression from the husband. Similarly, if the husband says to the wife 'Your command is in your hand' and she says 'I accept myself' intending divorce but not specifying a number, and then says 'I divorced myself three times', and the husband says 'I am saved' (najawtu), then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, no divorce occurs because the husband's statement 'I am saved' suggests he did not intend to delegate divorce; but according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), one divorce occurs because his response can be interpreted as mockery. The author then notes a difficulty: if the husband says 'Your command is in your hand' without intending any number and the wife divorces herself three times, why would one divorce not occur according to Imam Abu Hanifa? The passage then delves into the exception of the principle: expressions like 'You are irrevocably separated from me' (anti minni taliq) are suitable for the husband's divorce but not valid as the wife's response. The explanation is that in such phrases, the attribution of divorce is to the wife, not to the husband. The discussion from Jami' al-Fusulayn clarifies that certain kinayat (allusive expressions) like 'I joined my family' (alhaqtu nafsi bi ahli) are not effective without intention, unlike explicit phrases like 'You are forbidden' (anti haram) which are effective even without intention in the context of divorce discussion. The passage concludes with a note that the wife may be asked about her intention if there is no situational indication.

Key Terms
كناية (kinayah): An allusive expression that requires intention (niyyah) to effect divorce, unlike explicit terms.
نجوت (najawtu): The husband's statement 'I am saved' after the wife divorces herself; its interpretation affects the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the 'najawtu' case, what is the practical difference between the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions?
A) Abu Hanifa says one divorce occurs; the companions say three.
B) Abu Hanifa says no divorce occurs; the companions say one divorce occurs.
C) Both agree that no divorce occurs.
D) Both agree that one divorce occurs.

Key Takeaway
The wife's divorce after delegation requires careful analysis of the expression's attribution, and the husband's ambiguous response can cause an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Talaq — Divorce

Delegation of Divorce: The Phrase 'Amruk bi-Yadik'
Explains delegation of divorce and wife's kinaya responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on delegation of divorce using the phrase 'amruk bi-yadik', identify the conditions for its validity, and determine the number of divorces that fall.

Core Idea
Delegation of divorce is valid; the wife's authority is limited to one divorce in the council unless the husband intends more.

In Hanafi fiqh, a husband may delegate the right to divorce to his wife. This lesson examines the key phrase 'amruk bi-yadik' (your affair is in your hand) and its implications. The source text from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the phrase is not restricted to the word 'hand' and covers any similar expression. We will explore the conditions under which the wife can effect divorce, the number of divorces that result, and special cases such as the minor girl and conditional delegation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of delegation
The husband says 'amruk bi-yadik' or a similar phrase to delegate the power of divorce to his wife.
The phrase includes variants like 'fi yadik', 'fi kaffik', etc.
2
Determine the authority granted
The wife may divorce herself once in the same council unless the husband intended more. The relied-upon view is one divorce, regardless of the husband's unspoken intention.
Imam Muhammad holds that if the husband says 'three times', three divorces occur, but the mufta bihi is one.
3
Check the conditions
Is the wife adult and in a valid marriage? Is she in the same council? Was the delegation immediate or conditional?
For a minor girl, if she divorces herself, it occurs as if suspended.
4
Apply the ruling
If the wife then says a clear term like 'ana bain', one divorce occurs. If she uses kinaya, intention is needed.
The wife must respond in the same council; otherwise, the delegation lapses.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad counts three if the husband says 'thalath' or intends three; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold it is one unless explicitly specified.
The mufta bihi follows Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

She divorced, and if he said to her 'choose' and she said 'I have joined myself to my family' it does not occur, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn, and it is among the kinaya, so it is like her saying 'I am bain'. The ba in his saying 'your affair is in your hand' is not restrictive but problematic... And in al-Muhit from Muhammad: if he said 'three times your affair is in your hand' it is three, and if he said 'in your hand' then it is one. And 'hand' also is not restrictive, for if he said 'your affair is in your palms or your right or left or mouth or tongue' it is the same. Thus in al-Khulasa. And in al-Bazzaziyya: from the chapter on marriage of slave and freewoman: if he marries a woman on condition that she is divorced or that her affair is in her hand to divorce herself whenever she wants, the divorce does not occur nor is the affair in her hand. And if the woman initiated saying 'I married myself to you on condition that I am divorced or that my affair becomes in my hand to divorce myself whenever I want' and the husband said 'I accept', divorce occurs and the affair becomes in her hand. And if the slave initiated it is like the husband initiated; if the master initiated it is like the woman initiated. And in al-Bazzaziyya: if he said 'your affair is in your eyes' and the like, he is asked about intention; and 'my affair is in your hand' is like his saying 'your affair is in your hand'. And her claim against her husband that he made... Its opposite: his saying 'what is not so' etc. And it is refuted by its converse like 'you are from me divorced' for it is valid for effectuation from him despite not occurring if she answered with it, as the author mentioned. And it may be answered regarding the second that it is not valid for effectuation from him because her saying 'you are from me divorced' is kinaya for her saying 'my husband Zayd is from me divorced', so its counterpart would be 'I am from you divorced' not 'you are from me divorced', and thus it does not occur because it is kinaya for his saying 'your husband Zayd is from you divorced'. And thus is considered in its analogues: for in her saying 'you are to me haram' and the like it occurs, because if he said 'your husband Zayd is to you haram' or 'I am to you haram' it occurs because her saying 'you' is kinaya for the apparent. Similarly, if she said 'I divorced myself' it occurs because her saying 'myself' is expression for Zaynab for example, and if he said 'he divorced Zaynab' it occurs. And similarly her saying 'I am from you divorced' or 'I am divorced' occurs because if he attributed divorce to what she is from by her saying 'I' it occurs, unlike 'you are divorced' for if he attributed it to what he is from by it, it does not occur as we said. So it is not from me. The intent is expression by what one expressed but attribution of divorce to what one attributed it to, otherwise it would not occur in her saying 'I am from you divorced'. His saying: (It is problematic because it is from the kinaya...) I say: In the wording of Jami' al-Fusulayn is what repels the problem: its text: He said to his wife 'divorce yourself' and she said 'I am haram or khaliya or bariya or bain or batta or similar' so the principle is that everything from the husband is divorce if she asked him and he answered her with it; so if she implemented the like upon herself after the divorce became in her hand, she is divorced. So if she said 'divorce me' and he said 'you are haram or bain' she is divorced; so if he said it after the divorce became in her hand she is also divorced. So if she said to him 'divorce me' and he said 'join your family' and said 'I did not intend divorce' he is believed and she is not divorced; so if he said it after the divorce became in her hand such that she said 'I have joined myself to my family' she is also not divorced! And the explanation of that: 'I have joined myself to my family' is among the kinaya that are suitable for repudiation, so divorce does not occur by it except with intention, even in a state of anger or discussion of divorce, unlike 'haram bain' etc. For it occurs in a state of discussion without intention; so if she asked him for divorce and he said 'you are haram' it occurs without intention; so if she said it it also occurs, unlike 'join your family' for it is not determined for effectuation after her asking except with intention; so if she said it it does not occur. This is what appeared to me, so ponder it. His saying: 'he is asked about intention' that is if there is no contextual indication. Hence al-Maqdisi said after mentioning what passed that it is necessary... [PAGE_553] ...her affair in her hand is not accepted. But if she effected divorce by virtue of delegation then claimed the dower and divorce, it is heard, and she has no right to raise the matter to the judge until he compels the husband to place her affair in her hand. And in Talkhis al-Jami': if he said in sale and divorce 'her affair is in the hand of God and your hand' or 'sell as God wills and you will', the addressee acts alone because mentioning God is for blessing and facilitation by custom; and the ba is for consideration, so they are disregarded in it unlike the origin, such as 'as you wish' according to him, unlike 'if God wills' or 'what God wills and you will' when the origin is nullified or suspended on an unknown, according to the effect in 'if God wills you are divorced' so the conjunction is void and it is informative about reality. And if he said 'in my hand and your hand' or 'you will and I will' he does not act alone, construed as suspension because ownership is impossible. And in al-Muhit: if he said to his wife 'you are divorced' or 'your affair is in your hand', she is not divorced until she chooses herself in her council; then the husband is given choice: if he wishes he effects one divorce, and if he wishes he effects by her choice. And the woman addressed is absolute, so it includes the minor. So if he said to a minor 'your affair is in your hand' intending divorce, and she divorced herself, it occurs as if he suspended her divorce on her effectuation. Thus in al-Bazzaziyya. And 'the affair in hand' is absolute, including immediate and conditional when its condition is found. From it is what is in al-Muhit: if he said 'if you enter the house then your affair is in your hand' and she divorced herself as she placed her foot therein, she is divorced because the affair was in her hand; but if she divorced after walking two steps, she is not divorced because she divorced after the affair left her hand. And if he said 'your affair is in your hand regarding three divorces if you release me from your dower' and she said 'appoint me so that I divorce myself' and he said 'you are my agent to divorce yourself', then if she released him from the dower first then divorced in the council, she is divorced; if she did not release him, it does not occur because the agency was conditional on her releasing him from the dower. And from it is what is in al-Bazzaziyya: he said to her 'if I am absent from you and stay in my absence a day or two then your affair is in your hand', so this is upon the first of the two matters, so divorce occurs if he stays a day, if he was absent from her like that, so her affair is in her hand. Then he came at the end of the period and concealed herself until the period passed; some gave fatwa that the affair remained in her hand, and Imam Qadi Khan holds that if he knew her location and did not go to her, it occurs; if he did not know her location, not; and the correct is that it does not occur. He said in al-Khazana: And if the absence is from her, her affair does not become in her hand; and the difference of answers regarding the one who is entered upon and others: her affair does not become in her hand. And regarding the one entered upon, if he was in the city and did not come to her home until the period completed, then it becomes in her hand. He made her affair in her hand if he is absent from her three months and the maintenance does not reach her, then he sent her fifty if it was not the amount of her maintenance, it becomes in her hand. And if the maintenance was deferred and she gifted the maintenance to him and the period passed, the affair does not become in her hand due to the oath being lifted according to them two, contrary to the second Imam. And if he claims... from the intention religiously, or the situation indicates it judicially, and what is in al-Bazzaziyya is construed upon that. His saying: 'And if she divorced after having walked two steps' she is not divorced. Al-Maqdisi said in his commentary: and in al-Atabiyya: if she walked one step it is nullified. I say: the reconciliation is that what is in al-Atabiyya is construed as when her foot was above the threshold and the other foot entered with it, and what preceded is when she was outside the threshold, so by the first step she did not exceed the first entry, so by the second she exceeds and the affair leaves her hand. His saying: 'and others not'
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling on when a husband delegates divorce to his wife by saying 'amruk bi-yadik' (your affair is in your hand). The text clarifies that the phrase is not restricted to the word 'hand' but includes any limb. It discusses the wife's response using kinaya (ambiguous expressions). A key point: if the wife answers with a clear term like 'haram' or 'bain' after delegation, divorce occurs. But if she uses a kinaya like 'alhaqtu nafsi bi-ahli' (I have joined my family), it does not count as divorce unless she intended it, following the principle that such phrases are suitable for repudiation (radd) and require intention. The author cites Jami' al-Fusulayn to resolve an apparent contradiction regarding when the wife uses kinaya after requesting divorce. The text also covers cases with conditions (e.g., 'if you enter the house'), the ruling for a minor girl, and the effect of timing (e.g., immediate vs. after walking steps). Additionally, it addresses scenarios where the husband holds back, agency, and oaths. Throughout, the Hanafi position is presented, with references to Imam Muhammad's view on triple divorce by such delegation, and differences among later scholars like Qadi Khan. The commentary emphasizes that every scenario hinges on whether the wife's act falls within the scope of the delegation and whether the conditions are met.

Key Terms
الأمر باليد (al-amr bi al-yad): Phrase where husband delegates divorce authority to wife, literally 'your affair is in your hand'.
الكنايات (al-kinayat): Ambiguous expressions that can mean divorce but require intention or context.
التفويض (al-tafwid): Delegation of the right to divorce to the wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife 'amruk bi-yadik' in the morning. She says 'ana bain' in the afternoon in a different house. What is the ruling?
A) Divorce occurs
B) No divorce because not in the same council
C) Only if she intended
D) Divorce occurs but can be revoked

Key Takeaway
Delegation of divorce is valid; the wife must respond with a clear term in the same council, and only one divorce falls per the relied-upon view.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Wife's Responses: Sarih vs Kinaya in Delegated Divorce
Explains delegation of divorce and wife's kinaya responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sarih (clear) and kinaya (ambiguous) responses in delegated divorce and apply the rule on intention.

Core Idea
In delegated divorce, a clear answer effects divorce instantly; a kinaya answer requires intention.

After a husband delegates divorce, the wife's response determines whether divorce occurs. The source text from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq distinguishes between clear terms like 'ana bain' and kinaya like 'alhaqtu nafsi bi-ahli'. This lesson explores the principle from Jami' al-Fusulayn: everything from the husband that would be divorce if asked, when repeated by the wife after delegation, effects divorce. But for kinaya, intention is necessary. We will examine the different categories of responses and their legal effects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the delegation
The husband has delegated divorce authority to the wife using a phrase like 'amruk bi-yadik'.
The wife now has the power to divorce herself once in the council.
2
Identify the wife's response
Determine whether the wife's answer is a clear term (sarih) or a kinaya (ambiguous expression).
Clear terms include 'ana bain', 'ana haram', 'ana khaliya', etc. Kinaya include 'alhaqtu nafsi bi-ahli', 'ana bariya'.
3
Apply the rule for clear terms
If the wife says a clear term like 'ana bain', divorce occurs immediately without need for intention, even in a non-divorce context.
The text says these terms effect divorce in the context of discussing divorce without intention.
4
Apply the rule for kinaya
If the wife says a kinaya like 'alhaqtu nafsi bi-ahli', divorce occurs only if she intended divorce at the time.
Kinaya are suitable for repudiation (radd) and require explicit intention.
5
Consider the husband's parallel responses
If the husband says the same kinaya to his wife when she asks for divorce, he is believed if he denies intention, and no divorce occurs.
The principle is reciprocal: what is kinaya from the husband is also kinaya from the wife.

She divorced, and if he said to her 'choose' and she said 'I have joined myself to my family' it does not occur, as in Jami' al-Fusulayn, and it is among the kinaya, so it is like her saying 'I am bain'. The ba in his saying 'your affair is in your hand' is not restrictive but problematic... And in al-Muhit from Muhammad: if he said 'three times your affair is in your hand' it is three, and if he said 'in your hand' then it is one. And 'hand' also is not restrictive, for if he said 'your affair is in your palms or your right or left or mouth or tongue' it is the same. Thus in al-Khulasa. And in al-Bazzaziyya: from the chapter on marriage of slave and freewoman: if he marries a woman on condition that she is divorced or that her affair is in her hand to divorce herself whenever she wants, the divorce does not occur nor is the affair in her hand. And if the woman initiated saying 'I married myself to you on condition that I am divorced or that my affair becomes in my hand to divorce myself whenever I want' and the husband said 'I accept', divorce occurs and the affair becomes in her hand. And if the slave initiated it is like the husband initiated; if the master initiated it is like the woman initiated. And in al-Bazzaziyya: if he said 'your affair is in your eyes' and the like, he is asked about intention; and 'my affair is in your hand' is like his saying 'your affair is in your hand'. And her claim against her husband that he made... Its opposite: his saying 'what is not so' etc. And it is refuted by its converse like 'you are from me divorced' for it is valid for effectuation from him despite not occurring if she answered with it, as the author mentioned. And it may be answered regarding the second that it is not valid for effectuation from him because her saying 'you are from me divorced' is kinaya for her saying 'my husband Zayd is from me divorced', so its counterpart would be 'I am from you divorced' not 'you are from me divorced', and thus it does not occur because it is kinaya for his saying 'your husband Zayd is from you divorced'. And thus is considered in its analogues: for in her saying 'you are to me haram' and the like it occurs, because if he said 'your husband Zayd is to you haram' or 'I am to you haram' it occurs because her saying 'you' is kinaya for the apparent. Similarly, if she said 'I divorced myself' it occurs because her saying 'myself' is expression for Zaynab for example, and if he said 'he divorced Zaynab' it occurs. And similarly her saying 'I am from you divorced' or 'I am divorced' occurs because if he attributed divorce to what she is from by her saying 'I' it occurs, unlike 'you are divorced' for if he attributed it to what he is from by it, it does not occur as we said. So it is not from me. The intent is expression by what one expressed but attribution of divorce to what one attributed it to, otherwise it would not occur in her saying 'I am from you divorced'. His saying: (It is problematic because it is from the kinaya...) I say: In the wording of Jami' al-Fusulayn is what repels the problem: its text: He said to his wife 'divorce yourself' and she said 'I am haram or khaliya or bariya or bain or batta or similar' so the principle is that everything from the husband is divorce if she asked him and he answered her with it; so if she implemented the like upon herself after the divorce became in her hand, she is divorced. So if she said 'divorce me' and he said 'you are haram or bain' she is divorced; so if he said it after the divorce became in her hand she is also divorced. So if she said to him 'divorce me' and he said 'join your family' and said 'I did not intend divorce' he is believed and she is not divorced; so if he said it after the divorce became in her hand such that she said 'I have joined myself to my family' she is also not divorced! And the explanation of that: 'I have joined myself to my family' is among the kinaya that are suitable for repudiation, so divorce does not occur by it except with intention, even in a state of anger or discussion of divorce, unlike 'haram bain' etc. For it occurs in a state of discussion without intention; so if she asked him for divorce and he said 'you are haram' it occurs without intention; so if she said it it also occurs, unlike 'join your family' for it is not determined for effectuation after her asking except with intention; so if she said it it does not occur. This is what appeared to me, so ponder it. His saying: 'he is asked about intention' that is if there is no contextual indication. Hence al-Maqdisi said after mentioning what passed that it is necessary... [PAGE_553] ...her affair in her hand is not accepted. But if she effected divorce by virtue of delegation then claimed the dower and divorce, it is heard, and she has no right to raise the matter to the judge until he compels the husband to place her affair in her hand. And in Talkhis al-Jami': if he said in sale and divorce 'her affair is in the hand of God and your hand' or 'sell as God wills and you will', the addressee acts alone because mentioning God is for blessing and facilitation by custom; and the ba is for consideration, so they are disregarded in it unlike the origin, such as 'as you wish' according to him, unlike 'if God wills' or 'what God wills and you will' when the origin is nullified or suspended on an unknown, according to the effect in 'if God wills you are divorced' so the conjunction is void and it is informative about reality. And if he said 'in my hand and your hand' or 'you will and I will' he does not act alone, construed as suspension because ownership is impossible. And in al-Muhit: if he said to his wife 'you are divorced' or 'your affair is in your hand', she is not divorced until she chooses herself in her council; then the husband is given choice: if he wishes he effects one divorce, and if he wishes he effects by her choice. And the woman addressed is absolute, so it includes the minor. So if he said to a minor 'your affair is in your hand' intending divorce, and she divorced herself, it occurs as if he suspended her divorce on her effectuation. Thus in al-Bazzaziyya. And 'the affair in hand' is absolute, including immediate and conditional when its condition is found. From it is what is in al-Muhit: if he said 'if you enter the house then your affair is in your hand' and she divorced herself as she placed her foot therein, she is divorced because the affair was in her hand; but if she divorced after walking two steps, she is not divorced because she divorced after the affair left her hand. And if he said 'your affair is in your hand regarding three divorces if you release me from your dower' and she said 'appoint me so that I divorce myself' and he said 'you are my agent to divorce yourself', then if she released him from the dower first then divorced in the council, she is divorced; if she did not release him, it does not occur because the agency was conditional on her releasing him from the dower. And from it is what is in al-Bazzaziyya: he said to her 'if I am absent from you and stay in my absence a day or two then your affair is in your hand', so this is upon the first of the two matters, so divorce occurs if he stays a day, if he was absent from her like that, so her affair is in her hand. Then he came at the end of the period and concealed herself until the period passed; some gave fatwa that the affair remained in her hand, and Imam Qadi Khan holds that if he knew her location and did not go to her, it occurs; if he did not know her location, not; and the correct is that it does not occur. He said in al-Khazana: And if the absence is from her, her affair does not become in her hand; and the difference of answers regarding the one who is entered upon and others: her affair does not become in her hand. And regarding the one entered upon, if he was in the city and did not come to her home until the period completed, then it becomes in her hand. He made her affair in her hand if he is absent from her three months and the maintenance does not reach her, then he sent her fifty if it was not the amount of her maintenance, it becomes in her hand. And if the maintenance was deferred and she gifted the maintenance to him and the period passed, the affair does not become in her hand due to the oath being lifted according to them two, contrary to the second Imam. And if he claims... from the intention religiously, or the situation indicates it judicially, and what is in al-Bazzaziyya is construed upon that. His saying: 'And if she divorced after having walked two steps' she is not divorced. Al-Maqdisi said in his commentary: and in al-Atabiyya: if she walked one step it is nullified. I say: the reconciliation is that what is in al-Atabiyya is construed as when her foot was above the threshold and the other foot entered with it, and what preceded is when she was outside the threshold, so by the first step she did not exceed the first entry, so by the second she exceeds and the affair leaves her hand. His saying: 'and others not'
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling on when a husband delegates divorce to his wife by saying 'amruk bi-yadik' (your affair is in your hand). The text clarifies that the phrase is not restricted to the word 'hand' but includes any limb. It discusses the wife's response using kinaya (ambiguous expressions). A key point: if the wife answers with a clear term like 'haram' or 'bain' after delegation, divorce occurs. But if she uses a kinaya like 'alhaqtu nafsi bi-ahli' (I have joined my family), it does not count as divorce unless she intended it, following the principle that such phrases are suitable for repudiation (radd) and require intention. The author cites Jami' al-Fusulayn to resolve an apparent contradiction regarding when the wife uses kinaya after requesting divorce. The text also covers cases with conditions (e.g., 'if you enter the house'), the ruling for a minor girl, and the effect of timing (e.g., immediate vs. after walking steps). Additionally, it addresses scenarios where the husband holds back, agency, and oaths. Throughout, the Hanafi position is presented, with references to Imam Muhammad's view on triple divorce by such delegation, and differences among later scholars like Qadi Khan. The commentary emphasizes that every scenario hinges on whether the wife's act falls within the scope of the delegation and whether the conditions are met.

Key Terms
الأمر باليد (al-amr bi al-yad): Phrase where husband delegates divorce authority to wife, literally 'your affair is in your hand'.
الكنايات (al-kinayat): Ambiguous expressions that can mean divorce but require intention or context.
التفويض (al-tafwid): Delegation of the right to divorce to the wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a kinaya expression for divorce according to the text?
A) Ana bain
B) Ana haram
C) Alhaqtu nafsi bi-ahli
D) Anta taliq

Key Takeaway
Kinaya responses in delegated divorce require intention; clear terms effect divorce immediately.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Whose Word Is Taken in Disputes over Delegated Divorce?
Explaining various scenarios of delegation of divorce to wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the husband's word is taken regarding the occurrence of a condition for delegation, and the wife's word regarding receipt of maintenance, by analyzing a dispute scenario and identifying the correct ruling.

Core Idea
In delegated divorce, the husband denies the condition's occurrence? His word prevails; but the wife's word stands on receipt of maintenance.

The Hanafi school permits the husband to delegate the power of divorce to his wife through phrases like 'amruki bi-yadiki'. This delegation can be immediate or conditional. A critical legal question arises when the husband and wife disagree: Did the condition for delegation actually occur? The answer depends on the type of fact in dispute. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the nuanced Hanafi rule, distinguishing between the occurrence of a condition (husband's word) and the receipt of a right (wife's word). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the law of delegated divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed fact
Determine whether the husband and wife are arguing about the occurrence of a condition (e.g., husband sending maintenance) or about the receipt of a specific item. The rule differs based on this distinction.
The condition triggers the delegation; receipt is a separate factual claim.
2
Recall the Hanafi principle for disputes
The one who denies an obligation or occurrence is favored because the default is absence of that thing. In delegation disputes, the husband denies the condition's occurrence, so his word is taken regarding the condition.
This is consistent with the general principle: al-asl 'adam al-shart.
3
Recognize the exception for receipt
If the dispute is about whether the wife received maintenance or a specific right, her word is taken because she is the one who would know receipt, and the husband claims to have fulfilled a right. The correct position is that her word is taken regarding receipt.
This exception is supported by al-Dhakhira and al-Muntaqa.
4
Apply to a concrete scenario
Consider the case: husband says 'If I do not give you two dinars by the month, your matter is in your hand.' They differ on whether he gave. His word is taken on the giving (the condition), but if she denies receiving, her word on receipt. This dual rule is the mufta bihi.
The practical outcome: the wife may execute divorce only if she can prove the condition or if the husband admits it.

كتاب الطلاق/ فصل في الأمر باليد

أمرها بيدها لا يقبل، أما لو أوقعت الطلاق بحكم التفويض ثم ادعت المهر والطلاق يسمع وليس لها أن ترفع الأمر إلى القاضي حتى يجبر الزوج على أن يجعل أمرها بيدها. وفي تلخيص الجامع : لو قال في البيع والطلاق أمرها بيد الله وبيدك أو بع بما شاء الله وشئت ينفرد المخاطب لأن ذكر الله تعالى للتبرك وللتيسير عرفاً، والباء للعوض فألغيا فيه دون الأصل مثل كيف شئت عنده بخلاف إن شاء الله أو ما شاء الله وشئت إذا بطل الأصل أو علق بمجهول حسب التأثير في إن شاء الله أنت طالق فلغا العطف وهو أخبر عن واقع، ولو قال بيدي وبيدك أو شئت وشئت لم ينفرد حملاً على التعليق إذ تعذر التمليك اهـ. وفي المحيط : لو قال لامرأته أنت طالق أو أمرك بيدك لم تطلق حتى تختار نفسها في مجلسها فحينئذ يخير الزوج إن شاء أوقع تطليقة وإن شاء أوقع باختيارها .اهـ. وأطلق في المرأة المخاطبة فشمل الصغيرة، فلو قال للصغيرة أمرك بيدك ينوي الطلاق فطلقت نفسها يقع كأنه علق طلاقها بإيقاعها. كذا في البزازية. وأطلق الأمر باليد فشمل المنجز والمعلق إذا وجد شرطه ومنه ما في المحيط : لو قال إن دخلت الدار فأمرك بيدك فإن طلقت نفسها كما وضعت القدم فيها طلقت لأن الأمر في يدها، وإن طلقت بعد ما مشت خطوتين لم تطلق لأنها طلقت بعدما خرج الأمر من يدها. ولو قال أمرك بيدك في ثلاث تطليقات إن أبرأتين عن مهرك فقالت وكلني حتى أطلق نفسي فقال أنت وكيلتي لتطلقي نفسك فإذا أبرأته عن المهر أولاً ثم طلقت في المجلس طلقت، وإذا لم تبرئه لا يقع لأن التوكيل كان بشرط أن تبرئه عن المهر اهـ. ومنه ما البرازية : قال لها إن غبت عنك ومكثت في غيبتي يوماً أو يومين فأمرك بيدك، فهذا على أول الأمرين فيقع الطلاق لو مكث يوماً إن غاب عنها كذا فأمرها بيدها فجاء في آخر المدة فتوارت حتى مضت المدة أفتى البعض ببقاء الأمر في يدها والإمام قاضيخان على أنه إن علم بمكانها ولم يذهب إليها وقع وإن لم يعلم بمكانها لا، والصحيح أنه لا يقع .

وفي الخزانة : وإذا كانت الغيبة منها لا يصير أمرها بيدها واختلاف الأجوبة في المدخولة وغيرها لا يصير أمرها بيدها، وفي المدخولة لو كان في المصر ولم يجئ إلى منزلها حتى تمت المدة فيصير بيدها جعل أمرها بيدها إن غاب عنها ثلاثة أشهر ولم تصل إليها النفقة فبعث إليها بخمسين إن لم يكن قدر نفقتها صار بيدها ولو كانت النفقة مؤجلة فوهبت له النفقة ومضت المدة لا يصير الأمر بيدها لارتفاع اليمين عندهما خلافاً للإمام الثاني. وإن ادعى من النية ديانة أو يدل الحال عليها قضاء وما في البزازية يحمل على ذلك قوله (وإن طلقت بعدما شئت خطوتين) لم تطلق قال المقدسي في شرحه وفي العتابية : وإن مشت خطوة بطل. أقول : توفيقه أن ما في العتابية يحمل على ما إذا كانت رجلها فوق العتبة والأخرى دخلت بها وما سبق على ما إذا كانت خارج العتبة فبأول خطوة لم تتعد أول الدخول فبالثانية تتعدى ويخرج الأمر من يدها قوله: (وغيرها لا

وصول النفقة إليها وادعت حصول الشرط قيل القول قوله لأنه ينكر الوقوع لكن لا يثبت وصول النفقة إليها، والأصح أن القول قولها في هذا وفي كل موضع يدعي إيفاء حق وهي تنكر جعل أمرها بيدها إن لم يعطها كذا في يوم كذا. ثم اختلفا في الإعطاء وعدمه بعد الوقت فالقول له في حق عدم الطلاق، ولها في حق عدم أخذ ذلك الشيء. كذا في الذخيرة. وفي المنتقى : إن لم آتك إلى عشرين يوماً فأمرها بيدها يعتبر من وقت التكلم، فإذا اختلفا في الإتيان وعدمه فالقول له لأنه منكر كون الأمر بيدها وذكر محمد ما يدل على أن القول لها فيمن قال إن مات فلان قبل أن أعطيك المائة التي لك عليه فأنا كفيل به فمات فلان فادعى عدم الإيفاء وكونه كفيلاً وادعى المطلوب الإيفاء أن القول للطالب لأنه ينكر الاستيفاء وهذا استحسان قال لها قبل البناء لعدم الحضور لأن الغيبة قبل الحضور لا تمكن. قال لها إن لم أرسل نفقتك في هذا الشهر أو إن لم أبعث فأنت كذا، فأرسل إليها بيد رجل من يد الرسول لا يقع لأن البعث والإرسال قد تحقق. وإذا خافت المرأة إذا تزوجها أن لا يجعل الأمر بيدها بعد التزوج تقول زوجت نفسي منك بكذا على أن أمري بيدي أطلق منك نفسي متی شئت كلما ضربتني بغير جناية أو تزوجت علي أخرى أو تسريت أو غبت عني سنة جعل أمرها بيدها وهي صغيرة على أنه متى غاب عنها سنة تطلق نفسها بلا خسران يلحق الزوج فوجد الشرط فأبرأته عن المهر ونفقة العدة وأوقعت طلاقها يقع الرجعي ولا يسقط المهر والنفقة كما لو كان الإيجاب من الزوج موجوداً قبل وجود الشرط. قال لها أمر ثلاث تطليقات بيدك إن أبرأتيني عن مهرك إن قامت عن المجلس خرج الأمر من يدها، وإن أوقعت الطلاق في المجلس إن قدمت الإبراء وقع وإن لم تبرئه عن المهر لا يقع لأن التوكيل كان بشرط الإبراء. قال لها إن لم أعطك دينارين إلى شهر فأمرك بيدك فاستدانت وأحالت على زوجها إن أدى الزوج المال إلى المحتال قبل مضي المدة ليس لها إيقاع الطلاق، وإن لم يؤد ملكت الإيقاع. إن لم تصل إليك نفقة عشرة أيام فأمرك بيدك فنشزت بأن ذهبت إلى أبيها بلا إذنه في تلك الأيام ولم تصل إليها النفقة لا يقع لعدم وجوب النفقة فصار كما إذا طلقها حين تمت المدة. إن لم أوصل إليك خمسة دنانير بعد عشرة أيام فأمرك بيدك في طلاق متى فمضى الأيام ولم يرسل إليها النفقة إن كان الزوج أراد به الفور لها الإيقاع وإن لم يرد به الفور لا تملك الإيقاع حتى يموت أحدهما جعل أمرها بيدها إن ضربها بلا جناية فطلبت النفقة أو الكسوة وألحت لا يكون جناية لأن لصاحب الحق يد الملازمة ولسان التقاضي، ولو شتمته أو مزقت ثيابه أو أخذت لحيته فجناية وكذا لو قالت له يا حمار يا أبله أو لعنته، ولو شئت يصير أمرها بيدها) أي غير المدخولة وسيأتي قريباً وجهه قوله : ( والأصح أن القول قولها الخ) سيأتي تحرير هذه المسألة في باب التعليق عند قول المتن وإن اختلفا في وجود الشرط فالقول له .
Book of Divorce / Chapter on Placing the Matter in Her Hand

Her matter being in her hand is not accepted. But if she executed divorce by virtue of delegation and then claimed the dower and divorce, it is heard, and she may not refer the matter to the judge until the husband is compelled to place her matter in her hand. In Talkhis al-Jami': If he said in sale and divorce: 'Her matter is in the hand of Allah and your hand' or 'Sell for whatever Allah and you will', the addressee acts alone because mentioning Allah Almighty is for blessing and for ease according to custom, and the 'ba' is for consideration, so they are disregarded in it unlike the original, like 'as you wish'. Unlike 'if Allah wills' or 'whatever Allah and you will' when the original is invalidated or suspended on an unknown, according to the effect in 'if Allah wills, you are divorced', the conjunction is void and it informs of a reality. If he said 'In my hand and your hand' or 'You will and I will', he does not act alone, being construed as suspension since ownership is impossible. End quote. In al-Muhit: If he said to his wife 'You are divorced' or 'Your matter is in your hand', she is not divorced until she chooses herself in her session. Then the husband may, if he wishes, effect one divorce, or if he wishes, effect it by her choice. End quote. The woman addressed is absolute, so it includes a minor. If he said to a minor 'Your matter is in your hand' intending divorce, and she divorced herself, it occurs as if he suspended her divorce on her execution. Thus in al-Bazzaziyya. The placing of the matter in the hand is absolute, so it includes the immediate and the suspended when its condition exists. Among it is what is in al-Muhit: If he said 'If you enter the house, your matter is in your hand', and she divorced herself as she placed her foot in it, she is divorced because the matter is in her hand. If she divorced after she walked two steps, she is not divorced because she divorced after the matter left her hand. If he said 'Your matter is in your hand for three divorces, provided you release me from your dower', and she said 'Authorize me until I divorce myself', and he said 'You are my agent to divorce yourself', and she first released him from the dower and then divorced in the session, she is divorced. If she does not release him, it does not occur because the agency was conditioned on her releasing him from the dower. End quote. Among it is what is in al-Baraziyya: He said to her 'If I am absent from you and stay in my absence a day or two, your matter is in your hand.' This is upon the first of the two matters, so the divorce occurs if he stays a day, if he was absent from her thus. Her matter is in her hand, if she came at the end of the period and hid until the period passed, some gave a fatwa that the matter remains in her hand, and Imam Qadi Khan held that if he knew her location and did not go to her, it occurs; if he did not know her location, no; and the correct is that it does not occur.

In al-Khazana: If the absence was from her, her matter does not become in her hand; and differences of answers concerning a deflowered one and others: it does not become in her hand. Concerning the deflowered one if he was in the city and did not come to her home until the period ended, then her matter becomes in her hand. He placed her matter in her hand if he is absent from her for three months and maintenance does not reach her; he sent her fifty if it was not sufficient for her maintenance, then her matter becomes in her hand. If the maintenance was deferred, she gifted him the maintenance and the period passed, her matter does not become in her hand due to the oath being lifted according to them, contrary to the second Imam. If he claimed intention privately or the situation indicates it judicially, and what is in al-Bazzaziyya is interpreted accordingly. His saying: (And if she divorced after she walked two steps) she is not divorced. Al-Maqdisi said in his commentary: In al-Atabiyya: If she walked one step, it is invalid. I say: The reconciliation is that what is in al-Atabiyya is interpreted as when her foot is above the threshold and the other entered with it, and what preceded is when she was outside the threshold; with the first step she did not exceed the first entry, with the second she exceeds and the matter leaves her hand. His saying: (and others not

Reaching of maintenance to her and she claimed the occurrence of the condition; it was said that his word is taken because he denies the occurrence, but the reaching of maintenance to her is not established. The correct is that her word is taken in this and in every place where he claims fulfillment of a right and she denies. He placed her matter in her hand if he does not give her such-and-such on a certain day. Then they differed concerning giving and not giving after the time: his word is taken regarding non-occurrence of divorce, and her word regarding not taking that thing. Thus in al-Dhakhira. In al-Muntaqa: 'If I do not come to you within twenty days, your matter is in your hand' is reckoned from the time of speaking. If they differ about coming or not, his word is taken because he denies that the matter is in her hand. Muhammad mentioned what indicates that her word is taken concerning one who said 'If so-and-so dies before I give you the hundred that he owes you, I am guarantor for it'; so-and-so died, and he claimed non-fulfillment and being guarantor, and the claimant claimed fulfillment: the word is for the claimant because he denies fulfillment. This is istihsan. He said to her before consummation due to lack of presence because absence before presence is not possible. He said to her 'If I do not send your maintenance this month' or 'If I do not send, you are such', and he sent to her via a man; from the hand of the messenger, it does not occur because sending and dispatching have been realized. If the woman fears when he marries her that he will not place her matter in her hand after marriage, she says: 'I married myself to you for such-and-such on condition that my matter is in my hand; I divorce myself from you whenever I wish; whenever you strike me without a crime, or marry another wife over me, or take a concubine, or are absent from me for a year, her matter is in her hand.' And she is minor on condition that whenever he is absent from her for a year, she divorces herself without loss to the husband. So the condition exists, and she releases him from the dower and the maintenance during the waiting period and executes her divorce; the revocable divorce occurs, and the dower and maintenance are not dropped, just as if the offer from the husband existed before the condition. He said to her 'Your matter is in your hand for three divorces, provided you release me from your dower.' If she stands up from the session, the matter leaves her hand. If she executes the divorce in the session, if she presents the release, it occurs; if she does not release him from the dower, it does not occur because the agency was conditioned on release. He said to her 'If I do not give you two dinars by the month, your matter is in your hand.' She borrowed and assigned the debt to her husband. If the husband pays the money to the assignee before the period ends, she does not have the right to execute divorce; if he does not pay, she possesses the right to execute. 'If maintenance does not reach you for ten days, your matter is in your hand.' She becomes disobedient (nushuz) by going to her father without his permission during those days, and maintenance does not reach her; the divorce does not occur because maintenance is not obligatory, so it becomes as if he divorced her when the period ended. 'If I do not deliver to you five dinars after ten days, your matter is in your hand for a divorce whenever.' The days passed and he did not send the maintenance. If the husband intended immediacy by it, she may execute; if he did not intend immediacy, she does not possess execution until one of them dies. He placed her matter in her hand if he strikes her without a crime. She requests maintenance or clothing persistently; that is not a crime because the right-holder has the hand of insistence and the tongue of litigation. If she insults him, tears his clothes, grabs his beard, that is a crime. Similarly if she says to him 'O donkey, O fool', or curses him. And if you wish, her matter becomes in her hand) i.e., a non-deflowered one, and its explanation will come shortly. His saying: (And the correct is that her word is taken, etc.) will come with the analysis of this issue in the chapter on suspension at the statement of the text: 'And if they differ about the existence of the condition, his word is taken.'
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This is an expansion (sharh) from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, addressing the chapter on placing the matter in the wife's hand (amr bi yadiha) – a mechanism by which the husband delegates authority to the wife to divorce herself. The passage is a compilation of rulings from various Hanafi sources (al-Muhit, al-Bazzaziyya, al-Khazana, etc.) and intra-Hanafi differences. The author systematically explains when delegation is effective, its conditions, and its limitations. Key points include: (1) If the wife claims she divorced under delegation and also claims mahr, her claim is heard, but she cannot force the husband to delegate. (2) If the husband uses Allah's name alongside the wife's ('her matter is in Allah's hand and your hand'), the mention of Allah is for blessing only, and the wife acts alone. Similarly in sale. (3) If the husband says 'you are divorced or your matter is in your hand', divorce does not occur until she chooses in the same session; then the husband may effect one divorce or by her choice. (4) Delegation can be to a minor; if a minor wife divorces herself under delegation, it is valid. (5) Delegation can be immediate or conditional; if she divorces upon entering the house (as she places foot), divorce occurs, but if she takes two steps first, the delegation ends and divorce does not occur. (6) If delegation is conditioned on releasing the husband from mahr, she must release first and then divorce in the same session. (7) In cases of absence, the exact wording matters; if the husband says 'if I am absent for a day or two', it is interpreted by the first occurrence. (8) If the wife becomes absent herself, delegation does not take effect. (9) Differences regarding whose word is accepted: when the husband claims he sent maintenance and wife denies, the correct position is that her word is taken regarding receipt, but if they differ about whether the condition occurred, his word is taken because he denies the occurrence. (10) If the husband imposes a condition like 'if you become rebellious' or 'if you strike him without cause', such actions affect the wife's right to execute divorce. The author also reconciles apparent contradictions between different sources, e.g., regarding the threshold step. Overall, the passage demonstrates the Hanafi approach to contractual language and conditional delegation, emphasizing literal interpretation and the importance of the husband's spoken words.

Key Terms
أمرك بيدك (amruki bi-yadiki): A phrase used by the husband to delegate the authority to divorce to the wife, literally 'your matter is in your hand'.
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation of authority, specifically the husband giving the wife the power to divorce herself.
قول قوله (al-qawl qawlu): Whose statement is taken as credible in a dispute, i.e., whose word is accepted (usually the one denying an obligation or occurrence).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Muhit, if the husband says 'If you enter the house, your matter is in your hand' and she divorces herself after walking two steps inside, what is the ruling?
A) The divorce occurs because she entered the house and the condition is fulfilled.
B) The divorce does not occur because she delayed beyond the first step, and the delegation ended.
C) The divorce is valid only if she divorces while still standing at the threshold.
D) The divorce occurs only if her foot touches the floor inside the house.

Key Takeaway
In delegated divorce, the husband's word is taken on whether the condition for delegation occurred, but the wife's word is taken on whether she received a specific right, such as maintenance.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Rulings on Wife's Jinaayah (Insubordination)
Enumerates acts constituting jinaayah by wife with ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which specific acts constitute jinaayah by a wife and state the default ruling and exceptions according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A wife's acts of insubordination forfeit her rights, but not all reactive speech is jinaayah.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq enumerates the specific acts that constitute jinaayah (legal offense) by a wife, particularly in the context of delegated divorce (tafwid). The rulings distinguish between initiated and reactive acts, and the general rule is that any act of disobedience or disrespect that violates marital norms is jinaayah, with some exceptions. The passage also presents a Quranic proof and an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding mutual cursing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and its proof
The passage begins with mutual cursing. Note that some scholars argue it is not jinaayah because the wife is not the initiator, citing Quran 4:148 (exception for the wronged). The common view (al-‘āmmah) holds it is jinaayah. Identify the proof text and how it is used.
The Quranic verse is used to argue that the wronged person may speak evil, so a reactive curse may not be a crime.
2
List the specific acts of jinaayah
Go through each example: insulting back explicitly, insulting a stranger, uncovering face to non-mahram (with Qadi's dissent), speaking to a stranger or quarrelling audibly, leaving house after prompt dower, giving husband's property without permission, cursing his relatives, and saying 'my husband is not a man.' Note that each is ruled as jinaayah with possible conditions.
For uncovering face, al-Qadi dissents because the face is not 'awrah.
3
Identify exceptions and conditional rulings
Some acts are not jinaayah: e.g., getting angry over plain bread. For uncovering face, it is jinaayah only if done with a suspected person. For saying 'I will do it' in response to a prohibition, it is jinaayah only if the act is sinful. Distinguish the absolute and conditional cases.
The text uses 'in al-sahih' and 'qila' to indicate preferred and weaker views.
4
Analyze the Hanafi position and ikhtilaf
The passage shows an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf: some early scholars say mutual cursing is not jinaayah, but the common view (al-‘āmmah) and al-Zahiriyyah affirm it is. Identify the mufta bihi position and the basis for the ikhtilaf.
The ikhtilaf turns on whether the wife is considered a wronged party (the initiator is the husband) or not.
5
Apply the rulings to a new scenario
Consider a wife who, after being cursed by her husband, responds by insulting his entire family. According to the common view, is this jinaayah? Yes, because even reactive insults are jinaayah. But if she only curses back, some scholars exempt her. Apply the rule consistently.
The general principle: any act that violates marital respect is jinaayah unless excused by necessity or custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لعنها فلعنته قيل ليس بجناية لأنها ليست ببادئة قال الله تعالى لا يحب الله الجهر بالسوء من القول إلا من ظلم [النساء : ١٤٨] والعامة على أنه جناية لأنه لا قصاص فيه حتى الثاني جانياً. قال لها بليدة فقالت له بليد مثل ذلك فهو جناية منها إذا صرحت به. ولو شتمت أجنبياً كان جناية، وكذا لو كشفت وجهها لغير محرم لأنه لا يجوز النظر والكشف بلا ضرورة . وقال القاضي : لا يكون جناية لأنه ليس بعورة. ولو كلمت أجنبياً أو تكلمت عامداً مع الزوج أو شاغبت معه فسمع صوتها أجنبي فجناية وخروجها من البيت بعد إيفاء المعجل جناية في الأصح وقيل جناية مطلقاً. وإعطاؤها شيئاً من بيته بلا إذنه حيث لم تجر العادة بالمسامحة به جناية، وكذا دعاؤها عليه، وكذا قولها الكلبة أمك وأختك بعد قوله جاءت أمك الكلبة، وكذا قولها أزواج النساء رجال وزوجي لا، ولو دعاها إلى أكل الخبز المجرد فغضبت لا يكون جناية .اهـ. وصحح في الظهيرية ما عليه العامة من أن لعنها بعد لعنه جناية وفيها : والصحيح أنها إن كشفت وجهها عند من يتهم بها فهو جناية، ولو قال لها لا تفعلي كذا فقالت أفعل ؛ إن كانت قالت ذلك في فعل هو معصية فهو جناية وإلا فلا اهـ.
If she curses him and he curses her, it is said it is not a crime because she is not the initiator. Allah said: 'Allah does not like the public mention of evil except by one who has been wronged' [4:148]. The general public holds it is a crime because there is no retaliation; even the second is a criminal. If he said to her 'you are stupid' and she said to him 'stupid' similarly, it is a crime from her if she explicitly said it. If she insults a stranger, it is a crime. Likewise if she uncovers her face for a non-mahram because looking and uncovering without necessity is not permissible. Al-Qadi said: it is not a crime because the face is not 'awrah. If she speaks to a stranger or speaks intentionally with the husband or quarrels with him and a stranger hears her voice, it is a crime. Her leaving the house after payment of the prompt dower is a crime in the soundest view, and it is said absolutely a crime. Giving something from his house without permission where the custom does not tolerate it is a crime. Likewise her supplicating against him. Likewise her saying 'your mother is a bitch and your sister' after he said 'your mother came as a bitch.' Likewise her saying 'the husbands of women are men and my husband is not.' If he invites her to eat plain bread and she becomes angry, it is not a crime. End. In al-Zahiriyyah he corrected what the general public holds that her cursing after his cursing is a crime. In it: the correct position is that if she uncovers her face in the presence of someone with whom she is suspected, it is a crime. If he said to her 'do not do this' and she said 'I will do it,' if she said that regarding an act that is disobedience, it is a crime; otherwise no. End.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage is a set of rulings on what constitutes jinaayah (criminal insubordination) by a wife, particularly in the context of delegated divorce (tafwid). The author begins with the case of mutual cursing. Some scholars say it is not jinaayah because she is reacting, citing Quran 4:148 that permits speaking evil when wronged. The common view (al-‘āmmah) holds it is jinaayah because there is no qisas, and even the second party is a criminal. Then specific instances are listed: insulting back with the same word if explicit, insulting a stranger, uncovering face to non-mahram (al-Qadi dissents as face is not 'awrah), speaking to a stranger or quarrelling audibly, leaving house after prompt dower, giving husband's property without permission, cursing his mother/sister in response, and saying 'my husband is not a man.' Inviting to plain bread and anger is not jinaayah. The author then quotes al-Zahiriyyah affirming the common view on mutual cursing and qualifying the ruling on uncovering face: it is jinaayah only if done with a suspected person. Saying 'I will do it' in response to a prohibition is jinaayah only if the act is sinful. All rulings presume the context of tafwid or discipline.

Key Terms
جناية (jināyah): A legal offense by the wife that may affect her rights, listed in the context of tafwid
بادئة (bādi’ah): The initiator of an insult or crime
الشاغبة (al-shāghibah): Quarreling or raising voice with husband

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the common view (al-‘āmmah), if the husband curses the wife and she curses back, is that jinaayah?
A) No, because she is not the initiator.
B) Yes, because even the second party is a criminal.
C) It is always jinaayah regardless.
D) It depends on her intention.

Key Takeaway
The default Hanafi position is that a wife's reactive cursing and most other acts of disrespect are jinaayah, unless custom or necessity excuses them.

Activation of Tafwid: When Is the Wife's Proof Heard?
Resolves first tafwid case: proof of marriage when other woman is absent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which the wife's proof of the husband's marriage is heard in tafwid and identify the ruling when the other woman is absent.

Core Idea
In tafwid, the wife's proof is heard only if she is a litigant; absence of the other woman bars her claim.

The delegation of divorce (tafwid) is a conditional power: if the husband marries another, the wife may divorce herself. This lesson unpacks the first scenario in Jami‘ al-Fusulayn, where the wife attempts to prove the husband's marriage. The critical question is whether her evidence is admissible when the other woman is not present to defend herself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the tafwid condition
Locate the condition set by the husband: 'if he marries another'. This triggers the wife's authority.
Tafwid is valid only if the condition is clearly stated.
2
Analyze the wife's claim
She claims the husband married so-and-so, and the other woman is present (first variant) or absent (second variant). Distinguish the two cases.
Presence of the other woman changes the procedural posture.
3
Determine admissibility of evidence
When the other woman is present and confirms the marriage, the proof is heard. When she is absent, the wife is not a litigant in that claim, so her proof is not heard.
The principle: only a party to the claim can present evidence.
4
Apply the ruling
In the absence of the other woman, the wife's authority does not activate because the marriage is not proven. The soundest view (al-asahh) rejects her proof.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the other narration would hear it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي جامع الفصولين : فوض إليها أمرها إن تزوج عليها ثم ادعت على الزوج أنك تزوجت على فلانة وفلانة حاضرة تقول زوجت نفسي منه وشهد الشهود بالنكاح يصير الأمر بيدها، ولو كانت فلانة غائبة عن المجلس وبرهنت هذه أنك تزوجت فلانة علي وصار الأمر بيدي هل يسمع؟ فيه روايتان والأصح أنها لا تسمع لأنها ليست بخصم في إثبات النكاح عليها ا هـ . وفي الفصول واقعة : جعل أمرها بيدها إن تزوج عليها ثم وهبت امرأة نفسها منه بحضرة شهود وقبل هو فصارت امرأته وقال عنيت في التفويض التلفظ بلفظ التزوج هل يصدق حتى لا الأمر بيدها؟ قال أجاب بعض من تصدى للإفتاء بلا تحصيل الدراية والرواية إنه يصدق وهذا غلط محض وخطأ صرف وأجبت أنه لا يصدق ويصير الأمر بيدها لأن نية الخصوص في الفعل لا تصح إذ الفعل لا عموم له اهـ. وقد بحث فيه في جامع الفصولين فليراجع .
In Jami‘ al-Fusulayn: He delegated her authority if he marries another, then she claimed against the husband: 'You married over me so-and-so and so-and-so is present saying I married myself to him and the witnesses testified to the marriage' — then the authority becomes hers. If so-and-so was absent from the assembly and this woman proved that you married so-and-so over me and the authority became mine, would it be heard? There are two narrations; the soundest is that it is not heard because she is not a litigant in proving the marriage against her. End. In al-Fusul: a case: He placed her authority in her hand if he marries another, then a woman gave herself in marriage to him in the presence of witnesses and he accepted, so she became his wife. He said: 'I intended by the delegation the utterance of the word tazawwaj (marry).' Is he believed so that the authority is not in her hand? He said: 'Some who sat for ifta without acquiring knowledge and narration answered that he is believed, and this is pure error and sheer mistake. I answered that he is not believed and the authority becomes hers because intention of specificity in an act is not valid since the act has no generality.' End. It has been discussed in Jami‘ al-Fusulayn, so refer to it.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage is a set of two rulings from Jami‘ al-Fusulayn regarding tafwid (delegation of divorce). The first scenario: the husband delegated to the wife the authority to divorce herself if he marries another. She later claims he did marry, and the other woman is present stating she married herself (via a guardian? unclear). If witnesses confirm, the wife's authority is activated. However, if the other woman is absent and the wife alone proves the marriage, the soundest view is that her claim is not heard because she is not a party in proving that marriage against herself (i.e., she is not a litigant in that claim). The second scenario: the husband delegated authority if he marries another; then a woman offers herself in marriage and he accepts. He later claims that by 'tazawwaj' he meant the utterance of the word, not the contract. The author (probably the commentator) says this interpretation is invalid and he is not believed, because when an act is done (the marriage contract), it cannot be restricted by a hidden intention. The authority therefore passes to the wife. The author notes that this is contrary to some fatwas, and refers to further discussion.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation by the husband to the wife of the power to divorce herself upon a condition
خصم (khaṣm): Litigant, a party with standing in a legal claim

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first scenario from Jami‘ al-Fusulayn, why is the wife's proof not heard when the other woman is absent?
A) Because the wife is not reliable.
B) Because she is not a litigant in proving the marriage.
C) Because the condition was not valid.
D) Because the husband objected.

Key Takeaway
In tafwid, activation requires objective proof; the wife's claim alone is insufficient if she is not a litigant.

Hidden Intent in Tafwid: Does a Husband's Niyat al-Khusus Override?
Resolves second tafwid case: husband's hidden intention rejected.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects a husband's hidden intention to restrict tafwid and apply the rule that intention of specificity in an act is invalid because the act has no generality.

Core Idea
A husband's hidden intention to restrict tafwid does not affect the activation because an act has no generality to be restricted.

In the second tafwid case from al-Fusul, a husband tries to escape his delegation by claiming he only meant the word 'tazawwaj' as an utterance, not the actual marriage. The commentary exposes this as a corrupt fatwa and affirms the invalidity of such hidden intent. This lesson examines the principle behind the rejection: niyat al-khusus (specific intention) is ineffective in acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's claim
He delegated tafwid if he marries another. Later he marries, but says he intended by 'tazawwaj' only the utterance of the word, not the contract.
This is a claim of hidden intention (niyat al-khusus).
2
Analyze the nature of the act
The act (the marriage contract) is a single event. It does not have different general and specific meanings. Intention cannot restrict an act that has no generality.
The principle: 'الفعل لا عموم له' (the act has no generality).
3
Apply the rule
Since the act (marriage) cannot be restricted, the husband's hidden intention is irrelevant. The condition is fulfilled, and tafwid activates.
Attempting to restrict it would be like saying 'I intended the color blue when I said red.'
4
Evaluate the opposing fatwa
Some muftis believed the husband's intention, but the author condemns this as 'pure error'. The correct position is that the intention is not heard.
This shows the importance of objective interpretation in Shar‘i rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

In Jami‘ al-Fusulayn: He delegated her authority if he marries another, then she claimed against the husband: 'You married over me so-and-so and so-and-so is present saying I married myself to him and the witnesses testified to the marriage' — then the authority becomes hers. If so-and-so was absent from the assembly and this woman proved that you married so-and-so over me and the authority became mine, would it be heard? There are two narrations; the soundest is that it is not heard because she is not a litigant in proving the marriage against her. End. In al-Fusul: a case: He placed her authority in her hand if he marries another, then a woman gave herself in marriage to him in the presence of witnesses and he accepted, so she became his wife. He said: 'I intended by the delegation the utterance of the word tazawwaj (marry).' Is he believed so that the authority is not in her hand? He said: 'Some who sat for ifta without acquiring knowledge and narration answered that he is believed, and this is pure error and sheer mistake. I answered that he is not believed and the authority becomes hers because intention of specificity in an act is not valid since the act has no generality.' End. It has been discussed in Jami‘ al-Fusulayn, so refer to it.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage is a set of two rulings from Jami‘ al-Fusulayn regarding tafwid (delegation of divorce). The first scenario: the husband delegated to the wife the authority to divorce herself if he marries another. She later claims he did marry, and the other woman is present stating she married herself (via a guardian? unclear). If witnesses confirm, the wife's authority is activated. Key Terms
نية الخصوص (niyat al-khuṣūṣ): A specific intention that attempts to restrict the meaning of an expression to a particular instance
الفعل لا عموم له (al-fi‘l lā 'umūm lahu): The act has no generality; it is a singular occurrence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the husband's claim of intending only the word 'tazawwaj' not accepted?
A) Because the wife's testimony is stronger.
B) Because the witnesses contradict him.
C) Because intention of specificity in an act is invalid since the act has no generality.
D) Because the condition was already fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
A husband's hidden intention cannot restrict the activation of tafwid because the act of marriage is a single objective reality.
