Linguistic Nuance in Conditional Tafwid: 'Reach' vs 'Deliver'
Rulings on expiry of tafwid condition and wife's insult as response to threat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the conditions of 'not reach' (لم تصل) and 'not deliver' (لم أوصل) in a conditional delegation of divorce and state whether the authority remains or ceases upon the husband's return.

Core Idea
In conditional tafwid, the wording determines fulfillment: 'not reach' requires non-receipt; 'not deliver' requires non-delivery.

This passage from al-Sayrafiyyah presents two nearly identical conditional phrases for delegating divorce authority to the wife. The only difference is the verb: one uses the passive (reach), the other active (deliver). Yet the legal outcome is opposite. Understanding this nuance is key to correctly applying conditional tafwid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional phrases
Extract the two wordings from the passage: the first uses 'if my maintenance does not reach you' (لم تصل), the second uses 'if I do not deliver your maintenance' (لم أوصل).
These are the only variants discussed.
2
Analyze the first phrase (non-receipt)
The condition is based on the wife's receipt (wuṣūl). Since the husband returned after ten days, she presumably received or could have received the maintenance, so the condition was not fulfilled. Therefore, the authority does not remain.
The return itself does not guarantee receipt, but the condition is on non-receipt; if she did not receive during the ten days, the condition is fulfilled. The passage assumes she could receive upon return.
3
Analyze the second phrase (non-delivery)
The condition is on the husband's delivery (īṣāl). He did not deliver during the ten days, so the condition is violated (ḥanath), and the authority remains.
Even if the wife received from another source, the condition is based on his delivery.
4
Apply the same logic to the insult scenario
The condition is 'if he strikes her without a legitimate cause.' If she insults him (e.g., calling him son of a hired man or adulteress), his striking is a response to provocation, so the condition is deemed fulfilled (she caused it). She may then divorce herself.
A mild or true insult (like 'weaver') does not trigger the condition.
5
Derive the underlying principle
The rulings hinge on the precise wording of the condition and the causation. The first scenario focuses on the verb's subject (receipt vs delivery); the second focuses on whether the wife's action caused the condition to occur.
This principle is consistent across Hanafi conditional tafwid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الصيرفية : قال لها إن لم تصل نفقتي إليك عشرة أيام فأمرك بيدك فغاب عشرة أيام فحضر قال: لا يبقى الأمر بيدها بخلاف ما لو قال إن لم أوصل إليك نفقتك عشرة أيام والمسألة بحالها حيث يبقى الأمر بيدها لأن شرط جعل الأمر بيدها عدم الإيصال دون الوصول ولم يوجد الإيصال فيحنث ولو جعل الأمر بيدها إن ضربها بغير جناية شرعية فقالت له وقت الخصومة يا ابن الأجير يا ابن العواني فضربها وأنه كما قالت لها أن تطلق نفسها. ولو قالت له يا ابن النساج إن كان كما قالت أو لا يعير بهذا لا يكون يصير وأنفقت من ماله
In al-Sayrafiyyah: He said to her: 'If my maintenance does not reach you for ten days, your authority is in your hand.' He was absent for ten days then appeared. It is said: the authority does not remain in her hand, unlike if he said: 'If I do not deliver your maintenance to you for ten days' and the case remains the same — the authority remains in her hand because the condition for making the authority in her hand is non-delivery, not non-receipt, and delivery did not occur, so he has broken his oath. If he placed her authority in her hand if he strikes her without a legitimate cause, and she said to him at the time of dispute 'O son of a hired man, O son of a public woman,' and he struck her, then it is as she said: she may divorce herself. If she said to him 'O son of a weaver' if it is as she said or it is not a shame, it does not become... and she spent from his wealth.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage from al-Sayrafiyyah discusses two conditional tafwid scenarios. First: the husband says 'If my maintenance does not reach you for ten days, your authority is in your hand.' He is absent ten days but returns. The ruling: the authority does not remain, because the condition is non-receipt (not reaching her). Since she did receive (or could receive? Actually he returned, so perhaps she didn't need to receive; the condition is failing because the absence ended without triggering the condition? The text contrasts with the wording 'if I do not deliver' — in that case, the authority remains because the condition is non-delivery by him, and he did not deliver during the ten days. The nuance is in the verb: 'tasal' (reach) vs 'usil' (deliver). If he said 'if it does not reach you,' the condition is the failure of receipt; if he appears, the condition may not have satisfied. If he says 'if I do not deliver,' the condition is his action; he failed to deliver, so condition holds. Second: if he delegates authority on condition that he strikes her without legal cause, and during a dispute she insults him (calling him son of a hired man, etc.), and he strikes her, then the condition is met and she can divorce herself. However, if she calls him 'weaver,' it may not be a cause if it's true or not shameful, so the authority may not activate. The final part about 'أنفقت من ماله' seems cut off; likely she used his wealth, which may be relevant.

Key Terms
إيصال (īṣāl): Delivery (by the husband) of maintenance
حنث (ḥanath): Violation of an oath or condition; here, the husband breaks his condition by failing to deliver maintenance

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the husband says 'If my maintenance does not reach you for ten days, your authority is in your hand' and he is absent and returns on day ten, what happens to the authority?
A) The authority remains because he did not deliver.
B) The authority does not remain because the condition was non-receipt, and he returned.
C) The authority depends on her intention.
D) The authority passes to a judge.

Key Takeaway
The precise wording of a condition in tafwid determines whether the wife gains the authority to divorce; 'not reach' focuses on her receipt, 'not deliver' on his action.

Delegated Divorce (Tafwid): Ba'in Divorce, Timing, and Returning Authority
Explanation of tafwid rulings: ba'in divorce, timing, and radd al-amr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why delegated divorce results in a ba'in divorce, apply the timing rules for the wife's choice, and distinguish between returning authority by choosing the husband and mere rejection.

Core Idea
Delegation (tafwid) entitles the wife to ba'in divorce only; her choice is immediate unless conditioned, and her selection of the husband voids the delegation for that day.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies several key rulings on delegated divorce (tafwid). It explains that when a husband delegates the power to divorce to his wife, the resulting divorce is irrevocable (ba'in), not revocable (raj'i). It also addresses timing conditions, repetition limits, and the effect of the wife returning the authority by choosing her husband. Understanding these points is crucial for correctly applying the rules of tafwid in both classical and modern contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on a minor wife's divorce
The text begins by stating that a minor's divorce is revocable and her waiver of dower/maintenance is invalid because she lacks legal capacity. Understand that the ruling here protects the minor's rights.
This is based on the principle that a minor's actions are not legally binding in matters of financial rights.
2
Analyze why delegated divorce yields a ba'in divorce
When the husband says 'your authority is in your hand' and the wife says 'I divorce myself once' or 'I choose myself with one divorce,' she becomes divorced with one ba'in. The text explains that delegation (tafwid) only applies to ba'in divorce because the wife gains control over her own marital status, which is incompatible with revocable divorce where the husband retains the right to take her back.
The phrase 'myself' is a condition to ensure she is referring to herself, not someone else.
3
Examine timing and repetition rules
The text, quoting Jami‘ al-Fusulayn, states that if the husband says 'whenever you wish,' the wife may divorce at any time, but she cannot divorce more than once in one sitting. She can separate the three divorces across different settings. The words 'if' (in) and 'when' (mata) do not allow repetition. Also, if the husband says 'today and the day after tomorrow,' each day is treated as a separate delegation, and the night between is not included for the option.
The distinction between 'every time' (kullama) and 'if/when' affects whether the option can be exercised repeatedly.
4
Understand the effect of returning authority (radd al-amr)
If the wife says 'I choose you' or 'I choose my husband,' she is considered to have returned the authority, which voids her power for that day but preserves it for the next day. The term 'return' here means choosing the husband, not merely rejecting the delegation. The text clarifies that this applies only under tafwid, not when a third party is delegated.
The commentators resolve an apparent contradiction by explaining that in tafwid, choosing the husband terminates the delegation for the current period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: (It occurs as revocable and the dower and maintenance are not dropped) meaning because she is a minor so her waiver is not valid. His statement: (And if she returns the authority on her day, the authority is void that day and her authority is in her hand the day after tomorrow) meaning if she says to her husband 'I choose you' or 'I choose my husband,' then her ownership ends on the first day. What is meant by 'return' is choosing the husband, and what is meant by 'void' is termination. We qualified it because if she said 'I returned it,' it does not become void. Hence in al-Dhakhirah: If he placed her authority in her hand or in the hand of a third party, it becomes binding and cannot be returned by their repudiation. So there is no contradiction between their statement 'it is not returned by repudiation' and their statement here 'when she returns it becomes void.' The commentators have taken another path to remove the contradiction, namely that it is returned by repudiation in the case of delegation, but in
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It explains several rulings regarding delegated divorce (tafwid). First, it clarifies that if a wife who is a minor divorces herself, the divorce is revocable and she cannot waive dower or maintenance because a minor's waiver is invalid. Second, it explains that if the husband says 'your authority is in your hand' and the wife says 'I divorce myself once' or 'I choose myself with one divorce,' she becomes divorced with a single irrevocable (ba'in) divorce. The reasoning: because when the husband delegated three divorces, she owns the right to one as well; and delegation only applies to ba'in divorce, not raj'i. The phrase 'myself' is a condition to ensure she is referring to herself. Third, quoting Jami‘ al-Fusulayn: if the husband says 'whenever you wish,' she can choose at any time, but she cannot divorce more than once in one sitting—however she can separate the three divorces across different settings. The words 'if' and 'when' do not allow repetition. Fourth, the phrase 'Today and the day after tomorrow' means the delegation is for two separate days; the night between is not included because each day is a separate command. Fifth, if she 'returns the authority' by choosing her husband, her right ends for that day but she still has it for the next day. 'Return' here means choosing the husband, not the act of rejection. There is a nuance: if she merely says 'I return it,' the authority does not void; but the author explains that in tafwid, the return (by choosing the husband) terminates the delegation for that day. The commentators resolve an apparent contradiction with al-Dhakhirah by explaining that in tafwid, the return (choosing the husband) voids the delegation for the current period, whereas in general delegation to a third party, it is irrevocable.

Key Terms
بائن (bā’in): Irrevocable divorce; the husband cannot take the wife back without a new marriage
رد الأمر (radd al-amr): Return of authority by the wife by choosing the husband
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation; husband gives wife authority to divorce herself
رجعي (rajʿī): Revocable divorce; husband can take wife back during iddah without new marriage

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The delegation is void for that day only, and she retains the option for subsequent days if the delegation covers them.
C) She cannot choose her husband; she must divorce or stay silent.
D) The husband must explicitly revoke the delegation.

Key Takeaway
Delegated divorce (tafwid) results in a single ba'in divorce; the wife's choice is immediate unless conditioned, and her selection of the husband voids the delegation only for that day.

Wife's utterance of one divorce in delegated talaq
Explaining the ruling when wife says 'I divorced myself once' in delegated talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a wife says 'I divorced myself once' in delegated talaq and explain why it results in a bain divorce.

Core Idea
Delegated talaq gives the wife full control; saying 'one' still yields an irrevocable bain divorce.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a specific scenario in delegated talaq (tafwid). When a husband says 'Your affair is in your hand' intending three divorces, and the wife responds with 'I divorced myself once' or 'I choose myself with one divorce', the ruling is that one irrevocable divorce occurs. The reasoning hinges on the principle that delegation only applies to bain divorces. Let's walk through the legal analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the formula of delegation
The husband says 'amruki bi-yadiki' intending three divorces. This delegation gives the wife ownership of the right to divorce herself.
The husband's intent is relevant: here he intended three, so she owns three.
2
Determine the wife's response
The wife says either 'I divorced myself once' or 'I choose myself with one divorce'. Both are clear expressions of taking one divorce.
The word 'nafs' (herself) is a necessary condition for the divorce to occur.
3
Apply the rule of tafwid
Since the wife owns the full right (three divorces) by delegation, she can give any number up to three. Saying 'once' does not restrict her; she has the authority to give one.
This is because she 'owned' the three by tafwid, so she can give one.
4
Classify the type of divorce
The one divorce is bain (irrevocable) because in Hanafi law, tafwid (delegation) is only valid for bain divorces, not raj'i. The wife owns her own affair only when the divorce is irrevocable.
If it were raj'i, the husband would still have the right to take her back, contradicting the notion of full delegation.
5
Contrast with other formulas
The passage also notes that if the husband says 'kullama shi't' (whenever you wish), the wife may repeat divorces across sessions but not more than one per session. This contrasts with 'idha' or 'mata' which do not allow repetition.
Understanding these nuances helps in applying the correct ruling to different delegation wordings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وفي طلقت نفسي واحدة أو اخترت نفسي بتطليقة بانت بواحدة) يعني في جواب قول الزوج أمرك بيدك ينوي ثلاثاً لأن الواحدة صفة للطلقة باعتبار خصوص العامل كما أنها صفة للاختيارة في التي قبلها فإن خصوص العامل اللفظي قرينة خصوص المقدر فتقع الواحدة لأنها لما ملكت الثلاث بالتفويض ملكت الواحدة فكانت بائنة لأن التفويض إنما يكون في البائن لأنها به تملك أمرها وهو بالبائن لا بالرجعي. وأشار بذكر النفس إلى اشتراطه «طلقت أيضاً. وفي جامع الفصولين : قال أمرك بيدك كلما شئت فلها أن تختار نفسها كلما شاءت في المجلس أو في مجلس آخر إلا أنها لا تطلق نفسها في المجلس أكثر من واحدة دفعة واحدة، وأما تفريقها الثلاث في المجلس فلها ذلك بخلاف «إذا» و«متى» فإنه ليس لها التكرار ولا يتقيد بالمجلس كـ«كلما اهـ
His saying: (And 'I divorced myself once' or 'I choose myself with one divorce' – she becomes irrevocably divorced by one). Meaning in response to the husband's statement 'Your affair is in your hand' intending three, because 'one' is a description of the divorce considering the specificity of the operative word, just as it is a description of the choice in the previous case. For the specificity of the verbal operative word is a qarina (indication) of the specificity of the implicit, so one occurs because when she owned the three by delegation, she owned the one, so it became irrevocable because delegation only occurs in an irrevocable divorce, because by it she owns her affair, and it is by an irrevocable divorce, not by a revocable one. And he indicated by mentioning 'herself' the condition of 'she divorced' also. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If he said 'Your affair is in your hand whenever you wish', she may choose herself whenever she wishes in the session or in another session, except that she cannot divorce herself in the session more than one at a time. As for her separating the three in the session, she may do so, unlike 'if' and 'whenever', for she does not have repetition, nor is it restricted to the session, like 'whenever' (kullama). End quote.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling when a husband delegates divorce to his wife by saying 'your affair is in your hand' (amruki bi-yadiki) and she responds with 'I divorced myself once' or 'I choose myself with one divorce'. The author clarifies that if the husband intended three divorces by his delegation, the wife's utterance of 'one' does not restrict her to one; rather, because she owned the right to three by the delegation, she can give one, and that one becomes a bain (irrevocable) divorce. The reasoning is that delegation (tafwid) only applies to bain divorces, not raj'i, because through delegation she controls her own affair, which is only possible if the divorce is irrevocable. The mention of 'herself' (nafs) in the wife's statement is a condition for the divorce to occur. The passage then quotes from Jami' al-Fusulayn an elaboration on a variant where the husband says 'whenever you wish' (kullama shi't): she may choose whenever in the same or different sessions, but cannot give more than one divorce in a single session; however, she may separate the three divorces across sessions. This is contrasted with the words 'if' (idha) and 'whenever' (mata), which do not allow repetition and are not restricted to the session, unlike 'kullama' which implies repetition.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation by the husband to the wife of the right to divorce herself.
بائن (ba'in): A divorce that is irrevocable, meaning the husband cannot take her back without a new marriage.
أمرك بيدك (amruki bi-yadiki): A formula of delegation giving the wife authority to divorce herself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No divorce occurs because she said 'once' contradicting his intent.
B) One revocable (raj'i) divorce occurs.
C) One irrevocable (bain) divorce occurs.
D) Three irrevocable divorces occur.

Key Takeaway
In delegated talaq, the wife's utterance of 'one divorce' results in one irrevocable (bain) divorce because tafwid is only valid for bain divorces.

Ruling on 'Your affair is in your hand today and tomorrow'
Ruling on delegation of divorce with time-bound phrase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the delegation of divorce using the phrase 'today and tomorrow' and apply it to determine the wife's right after rejection.

Core Idea
The phrase 'today and tomorrow' is a single delegation, not separate opportunities.

When a husband says 'your affair is in your hand today and tomorrow', a common misunderstanding is that the wife gets two separate days to decide. The Hanafi school clarifies that this is a single continuous delegation, including the intervening night. Moreover, if the wife refuses to divorce on the first day, her right to divorce does not extend to the next day. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its legal classification
Recognize that the husband's statement is a tamlik (delegation) of the power to divorce, and that it is a single delegation, not multiple.
The phrase 'amruki bi-yadik' is a recognized formulation for tafwid al-talaq.
2
Determine the scope of the time period
Understand that 'today and tomorrow' includes the night between them, based on linguistic and customary usage. The conjunction 'wa' groups them into one continuous period.
The text argues that because it is a single tamlik, the night is automatically included.
3
Analyze the condition of the 'council of consultation'
Refute the claim that the night might interrupt an ongoing council; the delegation ends at the close of the day, ending any consultation.
The commentator rejects the notion that the night could arrive while the council is still in session, as the council would have ended.
4
Apply the consequence of rejection
If the wife chooses her husband (rejects divorce) on the first day, her ownership of the right ends; she cannot use it the next day.
This is the mufta bihi position as recorded in al-Walwalijiyya.
5
Note the fatwa position
The relied-upon fatwa is that the right does not carry over, and the delegation is a single continuous authority, not a renewable option.
There is no disagreement among the Hanafi authorities on this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وفي أمرك بيدك اليوم وغداً يدخل أي الليل لأنه تمليك واحد فإنه لم يفصل بيوم آخر فكان جمعاً بحرف الجمع في التمليك الواحد فهو كقوله أمرك بيدك في يومين وفي مثله تدخل الليلة المتوسطة استعمالاً لغوياً وعرفياً، فقول الشارح تبعاً للهداية «وقد يهجم الليل ومجلس المشورة لم ينقطع مردود لأنه ينقطع لأنه يقتضي دخول الليل في اليوم المفرد لذلك المعنى قوله : (وإن ردت في يومها لم يبق في الغد يعني إذا اختارت زوجها في يومها انتهى ملكها فلا تملك اختيارها نفسها بعد ذلك وعليه الفتوى كذا في الولوالجية.
His statement: 'And in your affair is in your hand today and tomorrow'—the night enters because it is a single delegation, since he did not separate it by another day, so it is a combination with the conjunction in a single delegation. So it is like his statement 'your affair is in your hand for two days' and in its like the intermediate night enters linguistically and customarily. So the statement of the commentator following al-Hidayah: 'The night may suddenly come while the council of consultation has not ended' is rejected because it ends, since it entails the entry of the night into the single day for that meaning. His statement: 'And if she rejects on her day, it does not remain for tomorrow' means when she chooses her husband on her day, her ownership ends, so she does not own choosing herself after that, and upon it is the fatwa; thus in al-Walwalijiyya.
Source pages p. 559

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when a husband says to his wife 'your affair is in your hand today and tomorrow.' The commentator clarifies that this phrase constitutes a single delegation (tamlik wahid) of the power to divorce, not two separate delegations. Consequently, the night between the two days is included within this single period, based on both linguistic usage and custom. The commentator refutes the claim that the council of consultation might extend into the night without interruption, arguing that the night's entrance ends the council. Regarding the effect of rejection: if the wife chooses her husband (i.e., refuses to divorce) on the first day, her right to divorce herself ends entirely, and she cannot later divorce herself on the following day. This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) as recorded in al-Walwalijiyya. The ruling highlights that the delegation is a single continuous authority, not a renewable option.

Key Terms
أمرك بيدك (amruki bi-yadik): A phrase used by a husband to delegate to his wife the power to divorce herself.
تمليك (tamlik): The act of granting ownership or control; here, the husband grants the wife ownership of the power to divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The night is excluded from the delegation.
B) The night is included as part of the single delegation.
C) The night is considered a separate period for divorce.
D) The wife cannot divorce during the night.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'today and tomorrow' constitutes a single continuous delegation of divorce authority; if the wife rejects on the first day, her right to divorce expires.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Wife's Rejection in Time-Limited Delegation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on time-limited delegation of talaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on whether a wife's rejection of divorce within a time-limited delegation extinguishes her right for the entire period, and apply the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Rejection on day one does not end the delegation – Abu Hanifa sees it as a continuous ownership.

This lesson examines a key difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his student Abu Yusuf regarding the effect of a wife's initial choice in a time-limited delegation of divorce. The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies how each imam views the nature of the delegated authority. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the Hanafi principles of continuous ownership versus immediate abandonment.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify what Abu Hanifa holds regarding the wife's right after she says 'I choose my husband' on the first day.
Abu Hanifa: the right remains for the rest of the period.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State Abu Yusuf's opposing position: that the wife's authority ends entirely for the whole period.
Abu Yusuf: rejection equals abandonment of the entire delegation.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the reasoning behind Abu Yusuf's view: that the delegation is a single offer accepted or rejected in full.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine Abu Hanifa's rebuttal: the delegation is a continuous ownership not voided by partial exercise of opinion.
The key concept is that the wife's authority is tied to the ongoing marriage, not exhausted by one choice.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the wife can still divorce on day two; under Abu Yusuf, she cannot.
Later Hanafi practice tends to favour Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قالت اخترت زوجي بطل خيارها في اليوم ولها أن تختار نفسها في الغد عند أبي حنيفة. وقال أبو يوسف: خرج الأمر من يدها في الشهر كله. ولو قال أمرك بيدك هذه السنة فاختارت نفسها ثم تزوجها لم يكن لها خيار في باقي السنة، ولو طلقها زوجها واحدة ولم يدخل بها ثم تزوجها في تلك السنة فلها الخيار عند أبي حنيفة لأن طلقات هذا الملك ما استوفيت بعد. وقال أبو يوسف : لا خيار لها لأنه إنما يكون في الملك وقد بطل.
And if she said 'I choose my husband,' her option in the day becomes void, and she has the right to choose herself on the next day according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: The authority leaves her hand for the entire month. And if he said 'your affair is in your hand this year,' and she chose herself, then he married her, she has no option in the remainder of the year. And if her husband divorced her once without consummation, then married her within that year, she has the option according to Abu Hanifa because the divorces of this ownership have not yet been exhausted. Abu Yusuf said: She has no option because it only exists in ownership, and it has become void.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the effect of a time-limited delegation of divorce. The scenario is when the husband says 'your affair is in your hand until the end of the month' or 'for this year.' If the wife initially rejects divorce by saying 'I choose my husband,' Abu Hanifa holds that her right to divorce herself remains for the remainder of the specified time (e.g., she can still divorce on the next day of the month). Abu Yusuf, however, holds that her rejection ends her entire authority for the whole period. This difference has practical implications. In another case, if the wife divorces herself once within the year, then remarries the husband, Abu Hanifa says she retains the option for the rest of the year because the divorces of that specific ownership are not yet exhausted (she may still have remaining divorces). Abu Yusuf argues that since the original ownership was dissolved by the divorce, the new marriage creates a fresh ownership, so the earlier delegation is void. The passage does not state which view is mufta bihi, but the reliance on Abu Hanifa's view is evident in many later works.

Key Terms
اختيار (ikhtiyar): The wife's act of choosing either to divorce herself or to remain with her husband.
طلاق الملك (talaq al-milk): The divorces available within a particular marriage (e.g., three in a non-mughallazah marriage).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a wife says 'I choose my husband' on the first day of a month-long delegation, can she divorce herself on the second day?
A) Yes, she retains the right for the rest of the month.
B) No, her authority ends entirely.
C) Only if she did not stand up from the session.
D) It depends on whether the husband remarries her.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on time-limited delegation hinges on whether the wife's initial rejection extinguishes her right for the entire period or only for that day; later practice favors Abu Hanifa's view.

Repetition of Delegation Creates Independent Authorities
Ruling on repetition creating multiple delegations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that repeating the delegation phrase with 'waw' creates separate delegations, and apply it to determine the number of delegations.

Core Idea
Repeating 'your affair is in your hand' twice with 'waw' creates two independent delegations, not one emphasized.

In Hanafi divorce law, a husband can delegate the power of divorce to his wife using specific phrases. A key question arises when he repeats the phrase: does it become mere emphasis or does it grant multiple authorities? This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that repetition with the conjunction 'waw' creates separate delegations, each with its own legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal context
The act is tafweed (delegation) in the context of talaq. Understand that this is a form of empowering the wife to divorce herself.
2
Classify the text
This passage is a ruling statement about the effect of repetition. It does not provide a proof (dalil), but states a clear rule.
3
Extract the condition
The text mentions 'ittihad al-amr' (unity of the matter) as a condition for a single delegation. Repetition breaches this condition.
The condition is that the phrase must be said once to be one delegation.
4
Derive the consequence
When repeated with 'waw', the result is multiple independent delegations (tafweedayn). Each delegation is separate.
The 'waw' is key; without it, repetition might be mere emphasis.
5
Apply to a scenario
If the husband says the phrase twice with 'waw', the wife has two separate options to divorce herself. She can use one, and still retain the other.
This has practical implications for counting divorces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد باتحاد الأمر باليد لأنه لو كرره بأن قال أمرك بيدك وأمرك بيدك أو جعلت أمرك بيدك وأمرك بيدك كانا تفويضين، لأن الواو فيهما من
And it is conditioned upon the unity of the authority in the hand, because if he repeats it by saying 'your affair is in your hand and your affair is in your hand' or 'I made your affair in your hand and your affair in your hand,' they become two delegations, because the waw in them is
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage states a clear ruling: if the husband repeats the phrase 'your affair is in your hand' using the conjunction 'and' (waw), it constitutes two separate delegations (tafweedayn) of the power to divorce. This means the wife receives two independent opportunities to divorce herself, each with its own rules. For example, if she divorces herself once using the first delegation, she still retains the second delegation for another divorce. The condition of 'unity of the authority' (ittihad al-amr) is key: if the husband intends a single delegation, he should not repeat the phrase; doing so splits the authority. The sentence is cut off in the OCR, but the meaning is clear from the context and other sources.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafweed): A delegation of authority from husband to wife to divorce herself.
يد (yad): Literally 'hand', but in this context means authority, power, or control.
واو (waw): The Arabic letter 'w', used as a conjunction meaning 'and'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'your affair is in your hand and your affair is in your hand', how many delegations does this create?
A) One delegation, as it is just emphasis.
B) Two separate delegations.
C) It depends on his intention.
D) It is invalid due to repetition.

Key Takeaway
Repeating the delegation phrase with 'waw' creates multiple independent delegations, not a single emphasized one.

Time-Bound Delegation and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Wife's Option
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of choosing husband or divorce on time-bound delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the wife's option in time-bound delegation and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
In time-bound delegation, Abu Hanifa preserves the option for the entire period; Abu Yusuf ends it upon any action by the wife or divorce.

This passage delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute about the scope of the wife's option (al-khiyar) when the husband delegates the right to divorce for a specific period (day, month, year). The central question is whether the wife can still exercise the option after she has chosen her husband or after being divorced and remarried within the same period. The disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf has significant practical implications for the wife's ability to dissolve the marriage later.

Guided Path
1
State the initial ruling
When a husband delegates the right to divorce for a specified time (day, month, year), the wife has the option to divorce herself during the remainder of that period.
The month is a lunar month (based on the new moon).
2
Identify the trigger events causing dispute
Two events trigger disagreement: (1) the wife chooses her husband ('I choose my husband'), and (2) the husband divorces her without consummation and then remarries her within the same period.
3
Follow Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds that the command is one for the entire period; using the option for one day does not exhaust it. After choosing husband, the option remains for the remaining days. After divorce and remarriage, the divorces of that ownership are not exhausted, so the option returns.
The key proof: 'The command is one and for the whole period.'
4
Follow Abu Yusuf's reasoning
Abu Yusuf argues that the option is only valid as long as the marriage ownership exists. Choosing the husband or being divorced breaks the ownership, so no option remains for the remainder of the period.
Abu Yusuf's proof: 'The option is only in ownership; when ownership ceases, the option ceases.'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the wife can divorce herself on any subsequent day within the period after the initial action; under Abu Yusuf, she loses the delegation entirely after the first action, even if she later remarries.
This difference affects real-life fatwa: a wife under Abu Hanifa has a second chance; under Abu Yusuf, she does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو عرف فقال هذا اليوم أو هذا الشهر أو هذه السنة كان لها الخيار في بقية اليوم أو الشهر أو السنة ويكون الشهر هنا على الهلال وذكر الولوالجي : إذا قال أمرك بيدك إلى رأس الشهر فلها أن تطلق نفسها مرة واحدة في الشهر لأن الأمر متحد ولو قالت اخترت زوجي بطل خيارها في اليوم ولها أن تختار نفسها في الغد عند أبي حنيفة. وقال أبو يوسف: خرج الأمر من يدها في الشهر كله ولو قال أمرك بيدك هذه السنة فاختارت نفسها ثم تزوجها لم يكن لها خيار في باقي السنة، ولو طلقها زوجها واحدة ولم يدخل بها ثم تزوجها في تلك السنة فلها الخيار عند أبي حنيفة لأن طلقات هذا الملك ما استوفيت بعد. وقال أبو يوسف : لا خيار لها لأنه إنما يكون في الملك وقد بطل، وقدمنا في باب إضافة الطلاق إلى الزمان أنه لو قال أمرك بيدك إلى عشرة أيام فالأمر بيدها من هذا الوقت إلى عشرة أيام تحفظ بالساعات . ولو قال أنت طالق إلى سنة يقع بعد السنة إلا أن ينوي الوقوع للحال والعتق كالطلاق، وقدمنا أنواعاً . من هذا الجنس وهي مذكورة هنا في الخلاصة والبزازية، والكل ظاهر إلا ما أن الإبراء إلى شهر كالطلاق إلا إذا قال عنيت بالإبراء إلى شهر التأخير إليه فحينئذ يكون تأخيراً إليه .اهـ. فإنه يقتضي صحة إضافة الإبراء وقد صرح في الكنز الإجارة أنه من قبيل ما لا تصح إضافته .
If he specified and said: this day, or this month, or this year, she has the option for the remainder of the day, month, or year. The month here is based on the lunar month. Al-Walwaliji mentioned: If he said, 'Your affair is in your hand until the beginning of the month,' she can divorce herself once during the month because the command is one. If she said, 'I choose my husband,' her option for the day is void, and she can choose herself the next day according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: The command leaves her hand for the entire month. If he said, 'Your affair is in your hand for this year,' and she chose herself, then he remarried her, she has no option for the remainder of the year. If her husband divorced her once without consummation, then remarried her within that year, she has the option according to Abu Hanifa because the divorces of this ownership have not been exhausted. Abu Yusuf said: She has no option because the option is only in ownership, and it has ceased. We have previously mentioned in the chapter on adding divorce to time that if he said, 'Your affair is in your hand for ten days,' the command is in her hand from this time for ten days, counted by hours. If he said, 'You are divorced for a year,' it occurs after the year unless he intends immediate occurrence. Emancipation is like divorce. We have previously mentioned types of this kind, and they are mentioned here in al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya. Everything is clear except that release from debt (ibra) added to a month is like divorce, unless he said: I meant by ibra to a month a deferment to it, then it is a deferment to it. This implies the validity of adding ibra to a time, while it has been explicitly stated in Kanz al-Daqaiq that ibra is of the type that cannot be added to time.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding time-bound delegation. The author first states the ruling: when a husband specifies a period (day, month, year) for the delegation, the wife's option to divorce herself persists for the remaining part of that period. For a month, it means a lunar month. Al-Walwaliji adds that if the husband says 'until the beginning of the month,' she can divorce herself only once in that month because the delegation is one. The core dispute is whether the wife loses her option entirely for the remainder of the period if she chooses her husband or if the husband divorces her and then remarries her. Abu Hanifa holds that the option remains for the rest of the time (e.g., she can choose herself the next day if she chose her husband today). Abu Yusuf holds that the option is extinguished for the entire period. Abu Hanifa argues that choosing the husband only uses the option for that day, and since the command is for the whole period, the remaining days are still available. For remarriage after divorce, Abu Hanifa says the divorce did not exhaust all the divorces of that ownership, so the option returns. Abu Yusuf says ownership was broken by divorce, so no option exists. The author then notes previous discussions about adding divorce to time and compares with ibra (release from debt), stating that ibra can be added to time only if intended as deferment, otherwise it is invalid—contradicting a statement in Kanz al-Daqaiq that ibra cannot be added to time.

Key Terms
الأمر باليد (al-amr bi al-yad): Delegation of the right to divorce to the wife, where she chooses whether to divorce herself or not.
الخيار (al-khiyar): The wife's option to divorce herself or choose her husband within the time frame of the delegation.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Release from a debt; here compared to divorce in terms of adding to a time period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a husband says 'Your affair is in your hand for this year' and the wife chooses her husband, what is her option for the remainder of the year?
A) She has no option for the rest of the year.
B) She can still choose herself on any remaining day of the year.
C) She can only choose herself the next day, but not beyond.
D) She can choose herself only after the husband divorces her and remarries her.

Key Takeaway
In time-bound delegation, Abu Hanifa's view that the option remains for the whole period is the more commonly relied-upon position, while Abu Yusuf's view is stricter and ends the delegation upon any action by the wife.

The Effect of Conjunction Letters on Delegation Count
Rule for repeating delegation with waw/fa vs. ja'altu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for when repeating the delegation phrase creates two delegations versus one, and apply it to given scenarios.

Core Idea
Repeating the delegation phrase with waw or fa creates two delegations, but using 'ja'altu' merges them into one.

In Hanafi divorce law, the delegation phrase 'amr bi al-yad' (your affair is in your hand) grants the wife a conditional right. But what if the husband repeats this phrase? Does repetition create multiple rights or just one? This lesson unpacks the critical distinction based on the conjunction used—a subtle grammatical point with significant legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the repetition pattern
Look at how the husband repeats the delegation: with 'waw' (and), 'fa' (then), or 'ja'altu' (I placed).
The pattern determines whether the second phrase is independent or explanatory.
2
Classify the conjunction type
Waw and fa used as pure conjunctions ('atf) make each phrase independent. In contrast, 'ja'altu' followed by the same phrase indicates a single act of delegation.
Hanafi usul holds that waw does not imply order, fa implies sequence, but both can indicate separate statements.
3
Apply the rule
If the repetition uses waw or fa (without 'ja'altu'), it is two delegations. If it uses 'ja'altu' (I placed your affair in your hand, then your affair is in your hand), it is one delegation because the second explains the first.
The analogy with 'ja'altuki taliqan fa-anti taliq' (I made you divorced, so you are divorced) confirms only one divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد باتحاد الأمر باليد لأنه لو كرره بأن قال أمرك بيدك وأمرك بيدك أو جعلت أمرك بيدك وأمرك بيدك كانا تفويضين، لأن الواو فيهما من العطف لا للجزاء، وكذلك لو قال أمرك بيدك فأمرك بيدك لأن الفاء هنا بمعنى الواو ولأنه لا يصلح تفسيراً. ولو قال جعلت أمرك بيدك فأمرك بيدك فهو أمر واحد لأن معناه صار الأمر بيدك بجعل الأمر بيدك كقوله جعلتك طالقاً فأنت طالق، أو قال قد طلقتك فأنت طالق طلقت واحدة ولو جمع بين تفويضين بالواو والفاء أو بغيرهما، فإن كان بغيرهما بأن قال أمرك بيدك طلقي نفسك فاختارت نفسها فقال لم أرد بالأمر الطلاق يصدق يمينه لأنه ما وصل قوله طلقي بالكلام المبهم لأنه لم يذكر حرف الوصل فكان كلاماً مبتدأ فلم يصر تفسيراً للمبهم ولو كان بالعطف كقوله أمرك بيدك واختاري فطلقي فاختارت لا يقع شيء لأنه عطف قوله فطلقي على التفريضين المبهمين فلا يكون تفسيراً كلاماً مبتدأ، وقولها اخترت لا يصلح جواباً له فلا يقع، وإن طلقت يقع واحدة رجعية لأنه يصلح جواباً له . قضاء لهما فبقي مع يمينه وكذا لو قال أمرك بيدك واختاري فاختاري فطلقي نفسك فاختارت نفسها طلقت ثنتين أنه لم يرد بالأمر باليد الثلاث لأنه أتى بالتفويضين المبهمين بالعطف وهو للاشتراك فصار طلقي تفسيراً لهما. وكذا لو قال اختاري واختاري أو قال أمرك بيدك وأمرك بيدك فطلقي نفسك فاختارت طلقت ثنتين، ولو قال أمرك بيدك اختاري فطلقي نفسك فاختارت نفسها وقال لم أرد به الطلاق يقع تطليقة بائنة بالخيار الأخير لأن قوله فطلقي» تأسير للأخير فقط . ولو قال أمرك بيدك فاختاري أو اختاري فأمرك بيدك فالحكم للأمر حتى إذا نوى بالثلاث يصح ، ن وإذا أنكر الثلاث وأقر بالواحدة يحلف لأن الأمر يصلح علة والاختيار يصلح حكماً لا علة فصار الحكم للأمر تقدم أو تأخر. وكذلك لو قال أمرك بيدك فطلقي نفسك أو طلقي نفسك فأمرك بيدك، ولو قال أمرك بيدك فاختاري فطلقي فاختارت بانت بواحدة بالأمر لأن قوله «فاختاري» تفسير للأمر ، وقوله «فطلقي» تفسير لقوله فاختاري». ولو قال أمرك بيدك فاختاري طلقي نفسك فاختارت لم يقع شيء إذا لم يرد بالأمر والتخيير طلاقاً، فإن طلقت نفسها وقعت رجعية وتمامه في المحيط، وسيأتي إن شاء الله الجمع بين التفويضن لأجنبي.
And it is restricted by the unity of the command (amr bi al-yad), because if he repeats it by saying 'Your affair is in your hand, and your affair is in your hand' or 'I placed your affair in your hand, and your affair in your hand,' they become two delegations, because the waw here is for conjunction, not for condition. Similarly, if he said 'Your affair is in your hand, then your affair is in your hand,' because the fa here is like waw and because it cannot serve as an interpretation. If he said 'I placed your affair in your hand, then your affair is in your hand,' it is one command because its meaning is: your affair became in your hand by my placing it in your hand, like his saying 'I made you divorced, so you are divorced'; or if he said 'I have divorced you, so you are divorced,' she is divorced once. If he combined two delegations with waw and fa or otherwise, if it is with other than these, such as saying 'Your affair is in your hand, divorce yourself' and she chose herself, and he said 'I did not intend divorce by the command,' he is believed on oath because he did not connect his saying 'divorce' to the ambiguous statement, as he did not mention a conjunction letter, so it was an independent statement, so it did not become an interpretation of the ambiguous. If it was with conjunction, like his saying 'Your affair is in your hand, and choose, so divorce yourself' and she chose, nothing falls because he conjoined his saying 'so divorce' to the two ambiguous delegations, so it does not become an interpretation but an independent statement, and her saying 'I choose' is not a suitable answer to it, so nothing falls. But if she divorced, one revocable divorce falls because it is a suitable answer. This is by way of judgment for both, so it remains with his oath. Similarly, if he said 'Your affair is in your hand, and choose, and choose, so divorce yourself' and she chose herself, she is divorced twice because he did not intend three by the command, because he brought two ambiguous delegations with conjunction, and conjunction indicates sharing, so 'divorce' became an interpretation of both. Similarly, if he said 'Choose and choose' or 'Your affair is in your hand and your affair is in your hand, so divorce yourself' and she chose, she is divorced twice. If he said 'Your affair is in your hand, choose, so divorce yourself' and she chose herself and said 'I did not intend divorce by it,' one irrevocable divorce falls by the last option because his saying 'so divorce' restricts only the last. If he said 'Your affair is in your hand, so choose' or 'Choose, so your affair is in your hand,' the ruling is for the command, so even if he intended three, it is valid. If he denies three and admits to one, he takes an oath because the command can serve as a cause and the choice as an effect, not a cause, so the ruling is for the command, whether it comes first or last. Similarly, if he said 'Your affair is in your hand, so divorce yourself' or 'Divorce yourself, so your affair is in your hand.' If he said 'Your affair is in your hand, so choose, so divorce yourself' and she chose, she becomes irrevocably divorced by one divorce because his saying 'so choose' is an interpretation of the command, and his saying 'so divorce' is an interpretation of 'so choose'. If he said 'Your affair is in your hand, so choose, divorce yourself' and she chose, nothing falls if he did not intend divorce by the command and the choice. If she divorced herself, one revocable divorce falls. The rest is in al-Muhit, and there will come, God willing, the combination of two delegations for a third party.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal analysis of how to interpret a husband's repeated delegation words. The key principle is that the delegation is one unless the wording indicates two. When the husband repeats the phrase 'Your affair is in your hand' using 'and' (waw) or 'then' (fa), it creates two separate delegations because these conjunctive letters are for joining independent phrases, not for emphasis or interpretation. However, if he says 'I placed your affair in your hand, then your affair is in your hand' (using ja'altu), this is treated as one delegation because the second phrase is explanatory of the first, like saying 'I made you divorced, so you are divorced'—only one divorce occurs. The author then discusses various scenarios with the wife making choices after combined phrases. For example, if the husband says 'Your affair is in your hand, divorce yourself' without a conjunction, and she divorces, he can claim he did not mean divorce by the initial phrase (the ambiguous 'amr') because it was not explicitly connected to the divorce command. But if he uses 'and choose so divorce yourself' and she chooses, no divorce occurs from her choice because 'choose' is not a direct divorce, but if she actually divorces, it is one revocable divorce. When conjunctions are used with two ambiguous delegations (like 'Your affair is in your hand and your affair is in your hand') and then he says 'so divorce yourself,' that command interprets both delegations, resulting in two divorces if she acts. The passage continues with many detailed cases, showing the importance of linguistic structure in determining the number of divorces. The final part discusses precedence: when 'command' and 'choice' are combined, the command governs; if he intends three divorces, it is upheld, and if he denies three, he takes an oath because the command is the cause and choice is the effect.

Key Terms
التفويض (al-tafwid): Delegation of the right to divorce; synonym for 'amr bi al-yad'.
الواو والفاء (al-waw wa al-fa'): Conjunction letters; waw for pure conjunction, fa for sequential conjunction.
العطف (al-'atf): Grammatical conjunction; determines if a phrase is independent or an interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'Your affair is in your hand, and your affair is in your hand' (with waw), how many delegations are created?
A) One delegation because it is repetition.
B) Two delegations because the waw indicates conjunction of separate phrases.
C) Three delegations by default.
D) None, because it is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Waw and fa create multiple delegations; 'ja'altu' creates one.

Combining Delegation and Choice: Precedence and Interpretation
Combined delegation and choice scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the number of divorces that occur when the husband combines delegation with choice or divorce commands, and identify when the husband's intent is considered.

Core Idea
When delegation and choice are combined, the command governs; precedence determines which phrase interprets the other, and the husband's denial requires an oath.

The previous lesson covered simple repetition. Now we explore more complex scenarios where the husband mixes delegation phrases ('amr bi al-yad') with other commands like 'choose' (ikhtari) and 'divorce yourself' (talliqi nafsaki). The interplay of conjunctions and word order determines whether the wife's actions produce one divorce, two, or none—and the husband's intent may be decisive in some cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components
Look for the delegation phrase and any additional commands (e.g., 'choose', 'divorce yourself'). Note how they are connected (with waw, fa, or without).
The connection type determines whether the latter interprets the former or stands alone.
2
Determine interpretation vs. independence
If the additional command is conjoined with waw or fa to the delegation, it may become an interpretation of the delegation. If not conjoined, it is an independent statement.
An independent 'divorce yourself' without conjunction gives the husband room to deny intent.
3
Apply the precedence rule
When 'command' and 'choice' are combined, the command takes precedence as the cause, and choice is the effect. The husband's intent (e.g., intent for three divorces) is upheld if he swears.
This rule applies regardless of order: even if 'choose' comes first, the command governs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد باتحاد الأمر باليد لأنه لو كرره بأن قال أمرك بيدك وأمرك بيدك أو جعلت أمرك بيدك وأمرك بيدك كانا تفويضين، لأن الواو فيهما من العطف لا للجزاء، وكذلك لو قال أمرك بيدك فأمرك بيدك لأن الفاء هنا بمعنى الواو ولأنه لا يصلح تفسيراً. ولو قال جعلت أمرك بيدك فأمرك بيدك فهو أمر واحد لأن معناه صار الأمر بيدك بجعل الأمر بيدك كقوله جعلتك طالقاً فأنت طالق، أو قال قد طلقتك فأنت طالق طلقت واحدة ولو جمع بين تفويضين بالواو والفاء أو بغيرهما، فإن كان بغيرهما بأن قال أمرك بيدك طلقي نفسك فاختارت نفسها فقال لم أرد بالأمر الطلاق يصدق يمينه لأنه ما وصل قوله طلقي بالكلام المبهم لأنه لم يذكر حرف الوصل فكان كلاماً مبتدأ فلم يصر تفسيراً للمبهم ولو كان بالعطف كقوله أمرك بيدك واختاري فطلقي فاختارت لا يقع شيء لأنه عطف قوله فطلقي على التفريضين المبهمين فلا يكون تفسيراً كلاماً مبتدأ، وقولها اخترت لا يصلح جواباً له فلا يقع، وإن طلقت يقع واحدة رجعية لأنه يصلح جواباً له . قضاء لهما فبقي مع يمينه وكذا لو قال أمرك بيدك واختاري فاختاري فطلقي نفسك فاختارت نفسها طلقت ثنتين أنه لم يرد بالأمر باليد الثلاث لأنه أتى بالتفويضين المبهمين بالعطف وهو للاشتراك فصار طلقي تفسيراً لهما. وكذا لو قال اختاري واختاري أو قال أمرك بيدك وأمرك بيدك فطلقي نفسك فاختارت طلقت ثنتين، ولو قال أمرك بيدك اختاري فطلقي نفسك فاختارت نفسها وقال لم أرد به الطلاق يقع تطليقة بائنة بالخيار الأخير لأن قوله فطلقي» تأسير للأخير فقط . ولو قال أمرك بيدك فاختاري أو اختاري فأمرك بيدك فالحكم للأمر حتى إذا نوى بالثلاث يصح ، ن وإذا أنكر الثلاث وأقر بالواحدة يحلف لأن الأمر يصلح علة والاختيار يصلح حكماً لا علة فصار الحكم للأمر تقدم أو تأخر. وكذلك لو قال أمرك بيدك فطلقي نفسك أو طلقي نفسك فأمرك بيدك، ولو قال أمرك بيدك فاختاري فطلقي فاختارت بانت بواحدة بالأمر لأن قوله «فاختاري» تفسير للأمر ، وقوله «فطلقي» تفسير لقوله فاختاري». ولو قال أمرك بيدك فاختاري طلقي نفسك فاختارت لم يقع شيء إذا لم يرد بالأمر والتخيير طلاقاً، فإن طلقت نفسها وقعت رجعية وتمامه في المحيط، وسيأتي إن شاء الله الجمع بين التفويضن لأجنبي.
And it is restricted by the unity of the command (amr bi al-yad), because if he repeats it by saying 'Your affair is in your hand, and your affair is in your hand' or 'I placed your affair in your hand, and your affair in your hand,' they become two delegations, because the waw here is for conjunction, not for condition. Similarly, if he said 'Your affair is in your hand, then your affair is in your hand,' because the fa here is like waw and because it cannot serve as an interpretation. If he said 'I placed your affair in your hand, then your affair is in your hand,' it is one command because its meaning is: your affair became in your hand by my placing it in your hand, like his saying 'I made you divorced, so you are divorced'; or if he said 'I have divorced you, so you are divorced,' she is divorced once. If he combined two delegations with waw and fa or otherwise, if it is with other than these, such as saying 'Your affair is in your hand, divorce yourself' and she chose herself, and he said 'I did not intend divorce by the command,' he is believed on oath because he did not connect his saying 'divorce' to the ambiguous statement, as he did not mention a conjunction letter, so it was an independent statement, so it did not become an interpretation of the ambiguous. If it was with conjunction, like his saying 'Your affair is in your hand, and choose, so divorce yourself' and she chose, nothing falls because he conjoined his saying 'so divorce' to the two ambiguous delegations, so it does not become an interpretation but an independent statement, and her saying 'I choose' is not a suitable answer to it, so nothing falls. But if she divorced, one revocable divorce falls because it is a suitable answer. This is by way of judgment for both, so it remains with his oath. Similarly, if he said 'Your affair is in your hand, and choose, and choose, so divorce yourself' and she chose herself, she is divorced twice because he did not intend three by the command, because he brought two ambiguous delegations with conjunction, and conjunction indicates sharing, so 'divorce' became an interpretation of both. Similarly, if he said 'Choose and choose' or 'Your affair is in your hand and your affair is in your hand, so divorce yourself' and she chose, she is divorced twice. If he said 'Your affair is in your hand, choose, so divorce yourself' and she chose herself and said 'I did not intend divorce by it,' one irrevocable divorce falls by the last option because his saying 'so divorce' restricts only the last. If he said 'Your affair is in your hand, so choose' or 'Choose, so your affair is in your hand,' the ruling is for the command, so even if he intended three, it is valid. If he denies three and admits to one, he takes an oath because the command can serve as a cause and the choice as an effect, not a cause, so the ruling is for the command, whether it comes first or last. Similarly, if he said 'Your affair is in your hand, so divorce yourself' or 'Divorce yourself, so your affair is in your hand.' If he said 'Your affair is in your hand, so choose, so divorce yourself' and she chose, she becomes irrevocably divorced by one divorce because his saying 'so choose' is an interpretation of the command, and his saying 'so divorce' is an interpretation of 'so choose'. If he said 'Your affair is in your hand, so choose, divorce yourself' and she chose, nothing falls if he did not intend divorce by the command and the choice. If she divorced herself, one revocable divorce falls. The rest is in al-Muhit, and there will come, God willing, the combination of two delegations for a third party.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal analysis of how to interpret a husband's repeated delegation words. The key principle is that the delegation is one unless the wording indicates two. When the husband repeats the phrase 'Your affair is in your hand' using 'and' (waw) or 'then' (fa), it creates two separate delegations because these conjunctive letters are for joining independent phrases, not for emphasis or interpretation. However, if he says 'I placed your affair in your hand, then your affair is in your hand' (using ja'altu), this is treated as one delegation because the second phrase is explanatory of the first, like saying 'I made you divorced, so you are divorced'—only one divorce occurs. The author then discusses various scenarios with the wife making choices after combined phrases. For example, if the husband says 'Your affair is in your hand, divorce yourself' without a conjunction, and she divorces, he can claim he did not mean divorce by the initial phrase (the ambiguous 'amr') because it was not explicitly connected to the divorce command. But if he uses 'and choose so divorce yourself' and she chooses, no divorce occurs from her choice because 'choose' is not a direct divorce, but if she actually divorces, it is one revocable divorce. When conjunctions are used with two ambiguous delegations (like 'Your affair is in your hand and your affair is in your hand') and then he says 'so divorce yourself,' that command interprets both delegations, resulting in two divorces if she acts. The passage continues with many detailed cases, showing the importance of linguistic structure in determining the number of divorces. The final part discusses precedence: when 'command' and 'choice' are combined, the command governs; if he intends three divorces, it is upheld, and if he denies three, he takes an oath because the command is the cause and choice is the effect.

Key Terms
التفويض (al-tafwid): Delegation; synonym for 'amr bi al-yad'.
التخيير (al-takhyir): Choice; giving the wife the option to choose, which may lead to divorce if so intended.
العلة والحكم (al-illah wa al-hukm): Cause and effect; the command is a cause, the choice is an effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She is divorced twice.
B) She is divorced once irrevocably.
C) Nothing falls unless she actually divorces.
D) She is divorced once revocably.

Key Takeaway
When command and choice are combined, the command governs; additional commands interpret the delegation in a chain.

Refinement of Dispute on Option Duration and Consecutive Time References in Divorce
Critical refinement of intra-Hanafi dispute and ruling on temporal references in divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct transmission of the intra-Hanafi dispute on the end of the delegated option and apply Muhammad's rule to divorce with consecutive time references, distinguishing between one or two divorces based on the interval.

Core Idea
The correct transmission: Abu Yusuf restricts the option's invalidation to the same session; Muhammad's view on consecutive time references depends on the interval.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq critically refines an earlier intra-Hanafi dispute about when a wife's delegated option ends. The commentator clarifies that the correct transmission gives Abu Yusuf a narrower view than some narrations claim. Then it introduces a ruling on divorce with two time references, where the Hanafi masters differ on whether one or two divorces occur. Understanding both issues sharpens your ability to navigate textual ambiguities and temporal conditions in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The core dispute is about when the wife's option (khayar) ends after she voices the command or says she does not choose divorce. Does it leave her hand completely or only become invalid in that session?
The issue arises in the context of tafwid al-talaq (delegation of divorce).
2
State the positions and correct transmission
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the command leaves her hand entirely. Abu Yusuf, according to the correct transmission, holds that it becomes invalid only in that session, not another. The commentator notes that some narrations reverse this, but the first is correct.
The correction is critical because it affects whether the wife can exercise the option later.
3
Apply the concept to consecutive time references
The passage then examines a different scenario: if the husband says 'You are divorced today and at the beginning of the month.' The ruling depends on the interval. If the beginning of the month is the next day (no gap), one divorce occurs (Muhammad). If there is a gap, two divorces occur. Abu Yusuf says two divorces regardless.
This shows how temporal proximity affects legal outcomes in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Determine the practical difference
Under Muhammad's view, a careful calculation of the calendar is needed. Under Abu Yusuf, the outcome is always two divorces. The commentator does not specify which is mufta bihi, but Muhammad's view aligns with careful linguistic analysis.
The practical consequence: number of divorces affects subsequent marital options.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وقال أبو يوسف خرج الأمر ) قال في التتارخانية : وفي الخانية أوردت الأمر . أو قالت لا أختار الطلاق خرج الأمر من يدها في قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد وعلى قول أبي يوسف يبطل الأمر في ذلك المجلس لا في مجلس آخر وفي بعض الروايات ذكر الخلاف على عكس هذا والصحيح هو الأول ا هـ. فما هنا من أنه حكاية الخلاف على غير الصحيح. وذكر في البدائع مثل ما مر غير لم يذكر التصحيح وقد قدمنا عبارته قوله : (فإنه يقتضي صحة إضافة الإبراء قال المقدسي في شرحه أقول بعد أن ذكر أنه تأجيل معنى وليس بإبراء محض لا يرد . ذلك . وفي الجامع : لو قال أنت طالق اليوم ورأس الشهر يقع واحدة قيل تأويله أن يكون رأس الشهر غداً، أما إذا كان بينهما حائل وقع طلاقان في وقتين. وقيل ما وقع في الجامع قول محمد وهو يعتبر الفاصل، وعند أبي يوسف تطليقتان. ولو قال أمرك بيدك اليوم فعن محمد إلى الغروب،
His statement: 'And Abu Yusuf said: the command leaves her hand'. He said in al-Tatarkhaniyya: And in al-Khaniyya: 'She utters the command, or says: I do not choose divorce, the command leaves her hand in the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. And according to Abu Yusuf, the command becomes invalid in that session, not in another session.' In some transmissions, the dispute is mentioned opposite to this, and the correct is the first. So what is here is that the narration of the dispute is incorrect. And he mentioned in al-Bada'i' similar to what has passed, except that he did not mention the correction. He has previously presented his statement: 'For it requires the validity of adding ibra' to time.' Al-Maqdisi said in his commentary: I say after he mentioned that it is a deferment in meaning and not a pure ibra', that does not apply. That. And in al-Jami': If he said 'You are divorced today and at the beginning of the month,' one divorce occurs. It is said its interpretation is that the beginning of the month is tomorrow. But if there is an interval between them, two divorces occur at two times. Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage serves as a critical refinement of the earlier dispute between Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf. The commentator (likely from al-Tatarkhaniyya) quotes that when the wife simply utters the command or says 'I do not choose divorce,' the option leaves her hand according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but Abu Yusuf restricts the invalidation to that same session only, not a separate session. The commentator notes that some transmissions reverse the dispute, but the correct version is the first one. Therefore, the narration given earlier in the text (which attributed a broader view to Abu Yusuf) is incorrect. Then the commentator refers to al-Maqdisi's clarification on the ibra' issue, though the text is somewhat garbled. Finally, a new ruling from al-Jami' is presented: if a husband says 'You are divorced today and at the beginning of the month,' the ruling depends on the time interval. If the beginning of the month is the next day (so no gap), only one divorce occurs. If there is a gap (like today is the 1st and beginning of next month), two divorces occur at different times. This is attributed to Muhammad, while Abu Yusuf says two divorces regardless. Also, if he says 'Your affair is in your hand today,' Muhammad says the option is until sunset. This shows the Hanafi attention to linguistic and temporal nuances.

Key Terms
المجلس (al-majlis): A sitting or session; the option may be restricted to the same session according to some views.
الفاصل (al-fasil): An interval or gap between two time references, such as between 'today' and 'beginning of month' when they are not consecutive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The correct transmission of Abu Yusuf's view restricts the option's invalidation to the same session, and in divorce with two time references, an interval (fasil) triggers two divorces under Muhammad's view.

Parsing Delegation Phrases in Divorce: The Effect of Connectors
Ruling on combining two delegations with different connectors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the legal effect of different connectors (waw, fa, or no connector) when a husband combines two delegations of divorce authority, and state the Hanafi ruling for each scenario.

Core Idea
The presence or absence of a conjunction between two delegations determines whether the second clarifies the first or stands alone, affecting the number and type of divorces.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq examines a common yet nuanced issue in Hanafi divorce law: how a husband's delegation of the power to divorce (tafwid) is interpreted when he uses multiple expressions. You will learn to parse these statements and apply the correct ruling based on the connector used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of delegation
Note that the husband may use 'amruki biyadik' (your command is in your hand) or 'ikhtari' (choose) and various combinations. Recognize the two delegations as mubham (ambiguous) phrases that require interpretation.
The first delegation is often the core; the second may or may not clarify it.
2
Examine the connector
Determine if there is a conjunction (waw, fa) or no connector between the two delegations. Also note the order. The connector type determines whether the second phrase can serve as an interpretation (tafsir) of the first.
If no connector, the second is an independent statement (kalam mubtada'). If connected by waw or fa, it may be an interpretation depending on the wording.
3
Assess the suitability of the interpretation
If connected, check whether the second phrase is a valid interpretation of the first. For example, 'talliqi nafsaki' (divorce yourself) is a valid interpretation of 'amruki biyadik', but 'ikhtari' (choose) alone is not a direct response.
The second phrase must fit as a specification of the first.
4
Consider the wife's action and the husband's claim
What did the wife do: choose (ikhtarat), divorce herself (tallaqat), or something else? Also, does the husband claim he did not intend divorce? The ruling depends on her action and his oath.
The husband is believed on oath if he denies intent, unless the wording is explicit.
5
Determine the outcome
State the number and type of divorces (one revocable, one irrevocable, two, etc.) based on the previous steps. The passage provides specific results for each scenario.
Review the text to ensure the correct outcome for the given combination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق فصل في الأمر باليد للعطف لا للجزاء، وكذلك لو قال أمرك بيدك فأمرك بيدك لأن الفاء هنا بمعنى الواو ولأنه لا يصلح تفسيراً. ولو قال جعلت أمرك بيدك فأمرك بيدك فهو أمر واحد لأن معناه صار الأمر بيدك بجعل الأمر بيدك كقوله جعلتك طالقاً فأنت طالق، أو قال قد طلقتك فأنت طالق طلقت واحدة. ولو جمع بين تفويضين بالواو والفاء أو بغيرهما، فإن كان بغيرهما بأن قال أمرك بيدك طلقي نفسك فاختارت نفسها فقال لم أرد بالأمر الطلاق يصدق يمينه لأنه ما وصل قوله طلقي بالكلام المبهم لأنه لم يذكر حرف الوصل فكان كلاماً مبتدأ فلم يصر تفسيراً للمبهم ولو كان بالعطف كقوله أمرك بيدك واختاري فطلقي فاختارت لا يقع شيء لأنه عطف قوله فطلقي على التفريضين المبهمين فلا يكون تفسيراً كلاماً مبتدأ، وقولها اخترت لا يصلح جواباً له فلا يقع، وإن طلقت يقع واحدة رجعية لأنه يصلح جواباً له . قضاء لهما فبقي مع يمينه وكذا لو قال أمرك بيدك واختاري فاختاري فطلقي نفسك فاختارت نفسها طلقت ثنتين أنه لم يرد بالأمر باليد الثلاث لأنه أتى بالتفويضين المبهمين بالعطف وهو للاشتراك فصار طلقي تفسيراً لهما. وكذا لو قال اختاري واختاري أو قال أمرك بيدك وأمرك بيدك فطلقي نفسك فاختارت طلقت ثنتين، ولو قال أمرك بيدك اختاري فطلقي نفسك فاختارت نفسها وقال لم أرد به الطلاق يقع تطليقة بائنة بالخيار الأخير لأن قوله فطلقي تفسير للأخير فقط . ولو قال أمرك بيدك فاختاري أو اختاري فأمرك بيدك فالحكم للأمر حتى إذا نوى بالثلاث يصح ، ن وإذا أنكر الثلاث وأقر بالواحدة يحلف لأن الأمر يصلح علة والاختيار يصلح حكماً لا علة فصار الحكم للأمر تقدم أو تأخر. وكذلك لو قال أمرك بيدك فطلقي نفسك أو طلقي نفسك فأمرك بيدك، ولو قال أمرك بيدك فاختاري فطلقي فاختارت بانت بواحدة بالأمر لأن قوله «فاختاري» تفسير للأمر ، وقوله «فطلقي» تفسير لقوله فاختاري». ولو قال أمرك بيدك فاختاري طلقي نفسك فاختارت لم يقع شيء إذا لم يرد بالأمر والتخيير طلاقاً، فإن طلقت نفسها وقعت رجعية وتمامه في المحيط، وسيأتي إن شاء الله الجمع بين التفويضن لأجنبي.
Book of Divorce: Chapter on Command by Hand. Because of conjunction, not for condition. Likewise, if he said 'Your command is in your hand so your command is in your hand' because the 'fa' here is in the sense of 'waw' and because it is not valid as an interpretation. And if he said 'I made your command in your hand so your command is in your hand' it is one command because its meaning is: the command becomes in your hand by making the command in your hand, like his saying 'I made you divorced so you are divorced,' or he said 'I have divorced you so you are divorced' - one divorce. And if he combined two delegations with waw or fa or other than them, if it was with other than them, such as saying 'Your command is in your hand; divorce yourself' and she chose herself and she said 'I did not intend divorce by the command,' he is believed on his oath because he did not connect his saying 'divorce' to the ambiguous speech because he did not mention a conjunction word, so it was an independent statement and thus did not become an interpretation of the ambiguous. And if it was by conjunction, like his saying 'Your command is in your hand and choose, so divorce' and she chose, nothing occurs, because he attached his saying 'so divorce' to the two ambiguous delegations, so it is not an interpretation and is an independent statement, and her saying 'I choose' is not a valid response to it, so nothing occurs; but if she divorced, one revocable divorce occurs because it is a valid response to it. Adjudication for them both, so it remains with his oath. Also, if he said 'Your command is in your hand and choose, so choose, then divorce yourself' and she chose herself, she divorces two, because he did not intend by the command of the hand the three (divorces) because he came with the two ambiguous delegations by conjunction, and conjunction is for sharing, so 'divorce' became an interpretation for both. Likewise, if he said 'choose and choose' or said 'Your command is in your hand and your command is in your hand, so divorce yourself' and she chose, she divorces two. And if he said 'Your command is in your hand; choose; so divorce yourself' and she chose herself and said 'I did not intend divorce by it,' one irrevocable divorce occurs by the last option, because his saying 'so divorce' is an interpretation of the last only. And if he said 'Your command is in your hand so choose, or choose so your command is in your hand,' the ruling is for the command, such that if he intended three (divorces) it is valid. And if he denies three and admits one, he takes an oath, because the command is valid as a cause and the choice is valid as a ruling not a cause, so the ruling is for the command whether it comes first or last. Likewise, if he said 'Your command is in your hand so divorce yourself, or divorce yourself so your command is in your hand.' And if he said 'Your command is in your hand so choose, so divorce' and she chose, she becomes separated by one (divorce) by the command, because his saying 'so choose' is an interpretation of the command, and his saying 'so divorce' is an interpretation of 'so choose'. And if he said 'Your command is in your hand so choose; divorce yourself' and she chose, nothing occurs if he did not intend divorce by the command and the option; but if she divorced herself, a revocable divorce occurs. Its completion is in al-Muhit, and will come, Allah willing, the combination of two delegations for a stranger.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a dense legal text (matn) that examines how a husband's delegation of divorce rights to his wife is affected when he expresses two delegations using different connectors. The passage type is a ruling_statement, specifically a parsing_exercise because it presents multiple scenarios for memorization and analysis. The author first clarifies that if the husband says 'amruki biyadik' (your command is in your hand) and then repeats the same phrase with 'fa' (so), it is treated as a single delegation because the 'fa' here is synonymous with 'waw' (and), not for sequence. If he says 'ja'altu amraki biyadik fa amruki biyadik' (I made your command in your hand so your command is in your hand), it is one command, analogous to 'ja'altuki taliqan fa anti taliq'. When combining two delegations, the ruling depends on the connector. If there is no conjunction (e.g., he says 'amruki biyadik talliqi nafsaki' without a connecting letter), the second phrase is an independent statement and does not interpret the first; if the wife chooses herself and he claims he did not intend divorce, he is believed on oath. However, if the two delegations are connected by a conjunction like 'waw' or 'fa', the second can become an interpretation of the first. For example, 'amruki biyadik wa-ikhtari fa-talliqi' – if she chooses, nothing occurs because 'fa-talliqi' is not a valid response to the ambiguous delegation; but if she divorces, one revocable divorce occurs because it is a valid response. Further, if the husband says 'amruki biyadik wa-ikhtari fa-ikhtari fa-talliqi nafsaki' and she chooses, she divorces twice because both ambiguous delegations are present. The author differentiates between cases where the command is the primary cause (ilt) and the choice is a ruling (hukm), giving precedence to the command whether it comes first or last. The passage ends by indicating that the full discussion will continue later regarding delegation involving a stranger.

Key Terms
الأمر باليد (al-amr bi al-yad): A delegation by the husband to his wife giving her authority to divorce herself or choose regarding divorce.
التفويض (al-tafwid): The act of delegating authority to the wife to decide her own divorce.
المبهم (al-mubham): Ambiguous or vague speech that requires interpretation to become effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says: 'Ja'altu amraki biyadik fa-amruki biyadik'. The wife divorces herself. How many divorces occur?
A) One revocable divorce.
B) One irrevocable divorce.
C) Two divorces.
D) Three divorces.

Key Takeaway
When a husband combines two delegations, the presence and type of conjunction determine whether the second phrase interprets the first, affecting the number of divorces that can occur.
